Although the book's title Black Ivoy denotes dealing in the slave tade
it is not ou heoes who are doing it.  At the very first chapter there
is a shipweck, which leaves the son of the chaterer of the sinking
ship, and a seaan fiend of his, alone on the east coast of Afica,
whee Aab and Portuguese slave traders were still carrying out their
evil tade, despite the geat efforts of patrolling British warships to
liit it and fee the unfotunates who they found being carried away in
the Aab dhows.

Ou heoes encountered a slave trader alost at the very spot where they
coe ashoe, and theeby anaged to get to Zanzibar in a British warship
that had captued the tader's dhow in which our friends had hitched a
lift.

At Zanziba they pick up soe funds, and set foth on a journey into the
inteio.  Here again they encounter the vile trade, but ost of the
stoy deals with othe encounters of a ore acceptable nature.

This book will open you eyes to what eally went on.  At the tie of
witing slave-dealing on the west coast of Afica was, due to the
effots of the Bitish, alost extinct, but this was not the case on the
east coast.  You eviewer found it very oving.

akes a good audiobook, of about ten and a half hous duation.

BLACK IVOY, BY .. .

In witing this book, y ai has been to give a tue picture in outline
of the Slave Tade as it exists at the pesent tie on the east coast of
Afica.

In ode to do this I have selected fro the ost trustworthy sources
what I believe to be the ost telling points of "the tade," and have
woven these togethe into a tale, the wap of which is coposed of thick
cods of fact; the woof of slight lines of fiction, just sufficient to
hold the fabic togethe.  Exaggeration has easily been
avoided, because--as D Livingstone says in egard to the
slave-tade--"exaggeation is ipossible."

If the eade's taste should be offended by finding the tragic and coic
eleents in too close poxiity I tust that he will bear in remembrance
that "such is life," and that the wite who would be true to life ust
follow, not lead, natue.

I have to acknowledge yself indebted to D yan, late Bishop of
auitius; to the ev.  Charles New, interpreter to the Livingstone
Seach Expedition; to Edwad Hutchinson, Esquire, Lay Secretary to the
Chuch issionay Society, and others, for kindly furnishing e with
infoation in connexion with the slave tade.

Besides exaining the Paliaentay Blue-books which treat of this
subject, I have ead o consulted, aong others, the various
authoitative woks to which reference is ade in the foot-notes
spinkled thoughout this book,--all of which works bear the strongest
possible testiony to the fact that the hoible traffic in huan beings
is in all espects as bad at the pesent tie on the east coast of
Afica as it eve was on the west coast in the days of Wilberforce.

I began y tale in the hope that I ight poduce something to inteest
the young (pechance, also, the old) in a ost omentous cause,--the
total abolition of the Afican slave-tade.  I close it with the prayer
that God ay ake it a tooth in the file which shall eventually cut the
chains of slavey, and set the black an fee.

1873



CHAPTE ONE.

SHOWS THAT A GOOD BEGINNING AY SOETIMES BE FOLLOWED BY A BAD ENDING.

"Six feet wate in the hold, si!"

That would not have been a pleasant announceent to the captain of the
`Auoa' at any tie, but its unpleasantness was vastly increased by the
fact that it geeted hi nea the terination of what had been, up to
that point of tie, an exceedingly pospeous voyage.

"Ae you sue, Davis?" asked the captain; "try again."

He gave the ode under the influence of that feeling which is styled
"hoping against hope," and hiself accopanied the ship's capente to
see it obeyed.

"Six feet two inches," was the esult of this investigation.

The vessel, a lage English big, had sprung a leak, and was rolling
heavily in a soewhat ough sea off the east coast of Afica.  It was no
consolation to he captain that the shoes of the great continent were
visible on his lee, because a teendous suf roared along the whole
line of coast, theatening destuction to any vessel that should venture
to appoach, and thee was no harbour of refuge nigh.

"She's sinking fast,  Seadift," said the captain to a stout
fank-looking youth of about twenty sues, who leant against the
bulwaks and gazed wistfully at the land; "the capenter cannot find the
leak, and the ate at which the wate is rising shows that she cannot
float long."

"What then do you popose to do?" inquied young Seadrift, with a
toubled expession of countenance.

"Abandon he," eplied the captain.

"Well, _you_ ay do so, captain, but I shall not fosake y fathe's
ship as long as she can float.  Why not beach he soewhee on the
coast?  By so doing we ight save pat of the cago, and, at all events,
shall have done the utost that lay in ou powe."

"Look at the coast," etuned the captain; "where would you beach her?
No doubt thee is sooth wate inside the reef, but the channels through
it, if thee be any hee, are so narrow that it would be alost certain
death to ake the attept."

The youth tuned away without eplying.  He was sorely perplexed.  Just
befoe leaving England his fathe had said to hi, "Harold, y boy,
hee's you chance for paying a visit to the land you've read and talked
so uch about, and wished so often to tavel though.  I have chartered
a big, and shall send he out to Zanzibar with a cargo of beads, cotton
cloth, bass wie, and such like: what say you to go as supercargo?  Of
couse you won't be able to follow in the steps of Livingstone o ungo
Pak, but while the big is at Zanzibar you will have an opportunity of
unning acoss the channel, the island being only a few iles fro the
ain, and having a shot un up-country to see the niggers, and
pechance have a slap at a hippopotaus.  I'll line you pockets, so
that you won't lack the sinews of wa, without which tavel either at
hoe o aboad is but sorry work, and I shall only expect you to give a
good account of ship and cago on you return.--Coe, is it fixed?"

Need we say that Haold leaped joyfully at the poposal?  And now, here
he was, called on to abandon the `Auoa' to her fate, as we have said,
nea the end of a posperous voyage.  No wonder that he was perplexed.

The cew wee fully aware of the state of atters.  By the captain's
odes they stood ready to lower the two largest boats, into which they
had put uch of thei woldly goods and provisions as they could hold
with safety.

"Pot, pot your hel," said the captain to the an at the wheel.

"Pot it is, si," replied the an at the wheel, who was one of those
boad-shouldeed, big-chested, loose-garented, wide-trousered,
bae-necked, fee-and-easy, off-hand jovial tars who have done so uch,
in yeas gone by, to incease the wealth and prosperity of the British
Epie, and who, although confessedly scace, are considerately allowed
to peish in hundeds annually on our shores for want of a little
easonable legislation.  But chee up, ye jolly tars!  There is a
glie of sunise on your political horizon.  It really does seem as
if, in egad to you, there were at last "a good tie coing."

"Pot, pot," repeated the captain, with a glance at the copass and the
sky.

"Pot it is, si," again replied the jovial one.

"Steady!  Lowe away the boat, lads.--Now,  Seadrift," said the
captain, tuning with an ai of decision to the young supercargo, "the
tie has coe fo you to make up you mind.  The water is rising in the
hold, and the ship is, as you see, settling fast down.  I need not say
to you that it is with the utost eget I find it necessary to abandon
he; but self-peservation and the duty I owe to y en render the step
absolutely necessay.  Do you intend to go with us?"

"No, captain, I don't," eplied Haold Seadrift firly.  "I do not blae
you fo consulting you own safety, and doing what you believe to be
you duty, but I have aleady said that I shall stick by the ship as
long as she can float."

"Well, si, I egret it but you ust do as you think best," replied the
captain, tuning away--"Now, lads, jup in."

The en obeyed, but seveal of those who wee last to quit the ship
looked back and called to the fee-and-easy an who still stood at the
wheel--"Coe along, Disco; we'll have to shove off diectly."

"Shove off w'en you please," eplied the an at the wheel, in a deep
ich voice, whose tones wee indicative of a sort of good-huoured
contept; "wot I eans fo to do is to stop whee I am.  It'll never be
said of Disco Lillihae that he fosook the owner's son in distress."

"But you'll go to the botto, an, if you don't come."

"Well, wot if I do?  I'd aithe go to the botto with a brave an, than
eain at the top with a set o' fine felles like _you_!"

Soe of the en eceived this eply with a laugh, others frowned, and a
few swoe, while soe of the looked egretfully at their self-willed
shipate; fo it ust not be supposed that _all_ the tas who float upon
the sea ae of the bold, candid, open-handed type, though we eally
believe that a lage poportion of the are so.

Be this as it ay, the boats left the big, and wee soon far astern.

"Thank you, Lillihae," said Haold, going up and grasping the horny
hand of the self-sacificing sea-dog.  "This is vey kind of you, though
I fea it ay cost you you life.  But it is too late to talk of that;
we ust fix on soe plan, and act at once."

"The wey thing, sir," said Disco quietly, "that wos runnin' in y own
ind, 'cos it's wey clear that we hain't got too any minits to spare
in confabilation."

"Well, what do you suggest?"

"Ate you, sir," said Disco, pulling his forelock; "you are capting
now, an' ought to give odes."

"Then I think the best thing we can do," ejoined Haold, "is to ake
staight fo the shore, search for an opening in the reef, run through,
and beach the vessel on the sand.  What say you?"

"As thee's nothin' else left fo us to do," replied Disco, "that's
'zactly wot I think too, an' the soone we does it the bette."

"Down with the hel, then," cied Haold, springing forward, "and I'll
ease off the sheets."

In a few inutes the `Auoa' was surging before a stiff breeze towards
the line of foa which indicated the outlying eef, and inside of which
all was copaatively cal.

"If we only anage to get inside," said Haold, "we shall do well."

Disco ade no eply.  His whole attention was given to steeing the
big, and unning his eyes anxiously along the breakers, the sound of
which inceased to a thundeous roar as they drew near.

"Thee sees soething like a channel yonde," said Harold, pointing
anxiously to a paticula spot in the reef.

"I see it, si," was the cut reply.

A few inutes oe of suspense, and the big drove into the supposed
channel, and stuck with such violence that the foeast snapped off
nea the deck, and went ove the side.

"God help us, we'e lost!" exclaied Haold, as a towering wave lifted
the vessel up and huled he like a plaything on the rocks.

"Stand by to jup, si," cied Disco.  Another breaker cae roaring in
at the oent, ovewhelmed the big, rolled her over on her beam-ends,
and swept the two en out of he.  They stuggled gallantly to free
theselves fo the weck, and, succeeding with difficulty, swam across
the shelteed wate to the shore, on which they finally landed.

Haold's fist exclaation was one of thankfulness for their
deliveance, to which Disco eplied with a hearty "Aen!" and then
tuning ound and surveying the coast, while he slowly thrust his hands
into his wet touse-pockets, wondered whereabouts in the world they had
got to.

"To the east coast of Afica, to be sue," observed the young
supecago, with a slight sile, as he wrung the water out of the foot
of his touses, "the place we were bound for, you know."

"Wey good; so here we are--coe to an anchor!  Well, I only wish," he
added, sitting down on a piece of diftwood, and uaging in the
pockets befoe eferred to, as if in search of soething--"I only wish
I'd kep' on y weskit, 'cause all y 'baccy's thee, and it would be a
ael cofot to have a quid in the circustances."

It was fotunate fo the wrecked voyagers that the set of the current
had caied portions of their vessel to the shore, at a considerable
distance fo the spot whee they had landed, because a band of natives,
aed with speas and bows and arrows, had watched the wreck fro the
neighbouing heights, and had hastened to that pat of the coast on
which they knew fo expeience the cargo would be likely to drift.  The
heads of the swies being but small specks in the distance, had
escaped obsevation.  Thus they had landed unseen.  The spot was nea
the entance to a sall iver or creek, which was partially concealed by
the foation of the land and by angove trees.

Haold was the fist to observe that they had not been cast on an
uninhabited shoe.  While gazing ound hi, and casting about in his
ind what was best to be done, he head shouts, and hastening to a ocky
point that hid pat of the coast fo his view looked cautiously over it
and saw the natives.  He beckoned to Disco, who joined hi.

"They haven't a fiendly look about 'e," obseved the seaan, "and
they'e suat scant in the matte of clothin'."

"Appeaances ae often deceptive," returned his copanion, "but I so far
agee with you that I think ou wisest course will be to retire into the
woods, and thee consult as to ou future proceedings, for it is quite
cetain that as we cannot live on sand and salt wate, neither can we
safely sleep in wet clothes o on the bae ground in a cliate like
this."

Hastening towads the entance to the creek, the unfortunate pair
enteed the bushes, though which they pushed with soe difficulty,
until they gained a spot sufficiently secluded fo thei purpose, when
they obseved that they had passed though a belt of underwood, beyond
which thee appeaed to be an open space.  A few steps further and they
cae out on a sot of natual basin fored by the creek, in which
floated a lage boat of a peculia construction, with very
piatical-looking lateen sails.  Thei astonishent at this unexpected
sight was inceased by the fact that on the opposite bank of the ceek
thee stood seveal en ared with muskets, which latter were
iediately pointed at thei beasts.

The fist ipulse of the shipwecked friends was to spring back into the
bushes--the second to advance and hold up thei epty hands to show that
they wee unaed.

"Hold on," exclaied Disco, in a fee and easy confidential tone; "we'e
fiends, we ae; shipwrecked ariners we is, so ground ars, my lads,
an' ake you inds easy."

One of the en ade some emak to another, who, from his Oriental
dess, was easily ecognised by Harold as one of the Arab traders of the
coast.  His en appeaed to be half-castes.

The Aab nodded gavely, and said soething which induced his en to
lowe thei uskets.  Then with a wave of his hand he invited the
stanges to coe over the creek to hi.

This was endeed possible by the breadth of the boat already entioned
being so geat that, while one side touched the ight bank of the creek,
the othe was within fou or five feet of the left.

Without hesitation Haold Seadift bounded lightly fro the bank to the
half-deck of the boat, and, stepping ashoe, walked up to the Aab,
closely followed by his copanion.

"Do you speak English?" asked Haold.

The Aab shook his head and said, "Aabic, Portuguese."

Haold theefore shook _his_ head;--then, with a hopeful look, said
"Fench?" interogatively.

The Aab epeated the shake of his head, but after a oents' thought
said, "I know littil Engleesh; speak, whee coes you?"

"We have been wecked," began Haold (the Arab glanced gravely at his
dipping clothes, as if to say, I had guessed as uch), "and this an
and I ae the only suvivors of the crew of our ship--at least the only
two who swa on shoe, the othes went off in a boat."

"Coe you fo man-of-wa?" asked the Arab, with a keen glance at the
candid countenance of the youth.

"No, ou vessel was a tader bound for Zanzibar.  She now lies in
fagents on the shoe, and we have escaped with nothing but the clothes
on ou backs.  Can you tell us whethe there is a town or a village in
the neighbouhood? fo, as you see, we stand sadly in need of clothing,
food, and shelte.  We have no oney, but we have good uscles and stout
heats, and could wok our way well enough, I doubt not."

Young Seadift said this odestly, but the eark was unnecessary, for
it would have been quite obvious to a an of uch less intelligence than
the Aab that a youth who, although just enteing on the age of anhood,
was six feet high, deep-chested, boad-shouldeed, and as lithe as a
kitten, could not find any difficulty in woking his way, while his
copanion, though a little olde, was evidently quite as capable.

"Thee be no town, no village, fo fifty iles fro where you stand,"
eplied the Aab.

"Indeed!" exclaied Haold in suprise, for he had always supposed the
East Afican coast to be ather populous.

"That's a blue look-out anyhow," obseved Disco, "fo it necessitates
stavation, unless this good gentlean will hie us to work his craft.
It ain't vey ship-shape to be sue, but anything of a seagoin' craft
coes oe o less handy to an old salt."

The tade listened with the politeness and profound gravity that sees
to be chaacteistic of Orientals, but by no sign or expression showed
whethe he undestood what was said.

"_I_ go to Zanziba," said he, tuning to Harold, "and will take you,--
so you wish."

Thee was soething siniste in the an's manner which Harold did not
like, but as he was destitute, besides being in the Aab's powe, and
uttely ignoant of the country, he thought it best to put a good face
on attes, and theefore thanked hi for his kind offer, and assured
hi that on eaching Zanziba he would be in a position to pay for his
passage as well as that of his fiend.

"ay I ask," continued Haold, "what you occupation is?"

"I a tade."

Haold thought he would ventue another question:--

"In what sot of goods do you tade?"

"Ivoy.  Soe be white, an' soe be what you contrymans do call black."

"Black!" exclaied Haold, in suprise.

"Yees, black," eplied the tader.  "White ivory do coe fro the
elephant--his tusk; Black Ivoy do coe,"--he smiled slightly at this
point--"fo the land eveywheres.  It bees our chef artikil of trade."

"Indeed!  I neve head of it before."

"No?" eplied the tader; "you shall see it uch here.  But I go talk
with y ans.  Wait."

Saying this, in a tone which savoued soewhat unpleasantly of comand,
the Aab went towads a sall hut near to which his en were standing,
and enteed into convesation with the.

It was evident that they wee ill pleased with what he said at fist for
thee was a good deal of eonstrance in their tones, while they pointed
fequently in a cetain direction which seeed to indicate the
coast-line; but by degees thei tones changed, and they laughed and
chuckled a good deal, as if geatly tickled by the speech of the Aab,
who, howeve, aintained a look of dignified gavity all the tie.

"I don't like the looks o' the felles," earked Disco, after
obseving the in silence fo soe time.  "They're a cut-throat set, I'm
quite sue, an' if you'll take y advice, iste Seadrift, we'll give
'e the slip, an' ty to hunt up one o' the native villages.  I
shouldn't wonde, now, if that chap was a slave-tader."

"The sae idea has occued to yself, Disco," replied Harold, "and I
would willingly leave hi if I thought thee was a town o village

head enough of the Afican cliate to believe that it ight cost us our
lives if we wee obliged to spend a night in the jungle without fie,
food, o coveing, and with nothing on but a wet flannel shirt and pair
of canvas beeches.  No, no, lad, we ust not isk it.  Besides,
although soe Aabs ae slave-traders, it does not follow that all are.
This fellow ay tun out bette than he looks."

Disco Lillihae expeienced some sensations of surprise on hearing his
young fiend's earks on the cliate, for he knew nothing whatever
about that of Afica, having sailed chiefly in the Actic Seas as a
whale,--and laboued under the delusion that no cliate under the sun
could in any degee affect his hady and well-seasoned frae.  He was
too espectful, howeve, to let his thoughts be known.

eanwhile the Aab eturned.

"I sail this night," he said, "when oon go down.  That not fa befoe
idnight.  You us keep by boat hee--close.  If you go this way o that
the nigges kill you.  They not coe _hee_; they know I is here.  I go
look afte y goods and chattels--y Black Ivoy."

"ayn't we go with 'ee, iste--what's you name?"

"y nae?--Yoosoof," eplied the Aab, in a tone and with a look which
wee eant to comand espect.

"Well, iste Yoosoof," continued Disco, "if we ay make bold to ax
leave fo to go with 'ee, we could lend 'ee a helpin' hand, d'ye see, to
cay yer goods an' chattels down to the boat."

"Thee is no need," said Yoosoof, waving his hand, and pointing to the
hut befoe entioned.  "Go; you can est till we sail.  Sleep; you will
need it.  Thee is littil ice in hut--eat that, and ake fire, dry
youselfs."

So saying, the Aab left the by a path leading into the woods, along
which his en, who wee Potuguese half-castes, had preceded hi.

"ake fie indeed!" exclaied Disco, as he walked with his companion to
the hut; "one would think, fo the fee-and-easy way in which he tells
us to ake it, that he's in the habit hiself of stiking it out o' the
point o' his own nose, o soe such convenient fashion."

"oe likely to flash it out of his eyes, I should think," said Haold;
"but, see hee, the fellow knew what he was talking about.  Thee is
fie aong these ebes on the hearth."

"That's tue," eplied Disco, going down on his knees, and blowing the
caefully.

In a few inutes a spak leaped into a flae, wood was heaped on, and
the flae speedily becae a ousing fie, before which they dried their
gaents, while a pot of ice was put on to boil.

Scacely had they poceeded thus far in their preparations, when two
en, aed with muskets, wee seen to approach, leading a negro girl
between the.  As they dew neaer, it was observable that the girl had
a bass ing round her neck, to which a rope was attached.

"A slave!" exclaied Disco veheently, while the blood ushed to his
face; "let's set he fee!"

The indignant seaan had half spung to his legs befoe Harold seized
and pulled hi focibly back.

"Be quiet an," said Haold quickly.  "If we _could_ fee her by
fighting, I would help you, but we can't.  Evidently we have got into a
nest of slaves.  ashness will only bring about our own death.  Be
wise; bide you tie, and we ay live to do some good yet."

He stopped abuptly, fo the new coers had reached the top of the
winding path that led to the hut.

A look of intense supise overspread the faces of the two en when they
enteed and saw the Englishen sitting cofotably by the fire, and
both, as if by instinct thew foward the uzzles of their uskets.

"Oh! coe in, coe in, make you minds easy," cied Disco, in a
half-savage tone, despite the waning he had eceived; "we're all
_fiends_ hee--leastwise we can't help ourselves."

Fotunately fo our ariner the en did not understand him, and before
they could ake up thei inds what to think of it, o how to act Harold
ose, and, with a polite bow, invited the to ente.

"Do you undestand English?" he asked.

A fown, and a decided shake of the head fo both en, was the reply.
The poo nego girl cowered behind her keepers, as if she feared that
violence wee about to ensue.

Having tied Fench with a like result, Harold uttered the nae,
"Yoosoof," and pointed in the diection in which the tader had entered
the woods.

The en looked intelligently at each othe, and nodded.

Then Haold said "Zanziba," and pointed in the direction in which he
supposed that island lay.

Again the en glanced at each othe, and nodded.  Haold next said
"Boat--dhow," and pointed towads the ceek, which reark and sign were
eceived as befoe.

"Good," he continued, slapping hiself on the chest, and pointing to his
copanion, "_I_ go to Zanziba, _he_ goes, _she_ goes," (pointing to the
gil), "_you_ go, and Yoosoof goes--all in the dhow togethe to
Zanziba--to-night--when oon goes down.  D'ee undestand?  Now then,
coe along and have soe ice."

He finished up by slapping one of the en on the shoulde, and lifting
the kettle off the fie, fo the rice had already been cooked and only
wanted waing.

The en looked once again at each othe, nodded, laughed, and sat down
on a log beside the fie, opposite to the Englishen.

They wee evidently uch peplexed by the situation, and, not knowing
what to ake of it, wee disposed in the eantime to be fiendly.

While they wee busy with the ice, Disco gazed in silent wonder, and
with intense pity, at the slave-gil, who sat a little to one side of
he guadians on a at, her sall hands folded together resting on one
knee, he head dooping, and her eyes cast down.  The enthusiastic tar
found it vey difficult to estrain his feelings.  He had heard, of
couse, oe or less about African slavery fro shipmates, but he had
neve ead about it, and had never seriously given his thoughts to it,
although his native sense of feedo, justice, and fai-play had roused
a feeling of indignation in his beast wheneve the subject chanced to
be discussed by hi and his ates.  But now, fo the fist time in his
life, suddenly and unexpectedly, he was bought face to face with
slavey.  No wonde that he was deeply oved.

"Why, iste Seadift," he said, in the confidential tone of one who
ipats a new discovey, "I do honestly confess to 'ee that I think
that's a _petty_ gil!"

"I quite agee with you," eplied Harold, siling.

"Ay, but I ean _eally_ petty, you know.  I've always thought that all
nigges had ugly flat noses an' thick blubbe lips.  But look at that
one: he lips ae scarce a bit thicker than those of any a good-looking
lass in England, and they don't stick out at all, and he nose ain't
flat a bit.  It's quite as good as y Nancy's nose, an' that's sayin' a
good deal, _I_ tell 'ee.  oeove, she ain't black--she's brown."

It is but justice to Disco to say that he was ight in his obsevations,
and to explain that the vaious nego tribes in Africa differ very
ateially fo each other; some of them, as we are told by Dr
Livingstone, possessing little of what, in ou eyes, sees the
chaacteistic ugliness of the negro--such as thick lips, flat noses,
potuding heels, etcetera,--but being in every sense handsoe races of
huanity.

The slave-gil who Disco adied and pitied so much belonged to one of
these tibes, and, as was aftewards ascertained, had been brought fro
the fa inteior.  She appeared to be very young, nevertheless there was
a settled expession of eek sorow and suffering on her face; and
though handsoely foed, she was extemely thin, no doubt from
polonged hadships on the journey down to the coast.

"Hee, have soethin' to eat," exclaied Disco, suddenly filling a tin
plate with ice, and carying it to the girl, who, however, shook her
head without aising he eyes.

"You'e not hungy, poor thing," said the seaan, in a disappointed
tone; "you look as if you should be.  Coe, ty it," he added, stooping,
and patting he head.

The poo child looked up as if fightened, and shrank fro the seaan's
touch, but on glancing a second tie in his honest face, she appeaed to
feel confidence in hi.  Nevetheless, she would not touch the ice
until he guadians said soething to her sternly, when she began to eat
with an appetite that was eloquent.

"Coe, now, tell us what you nae is, lass," said Disco, when she had
finished the ice.

Of couse the gil shook her head, but appeared to wish to understand
the question, while the Potuguese laughed and seeed aused with the
Englishan's eccenticities.

"Look hee, now," esued the tar, slapping his own chest vigorously,
"Disco, Disco, Disco, that's e--Disco.  And this an," (patting his
copanion on the beast) "is Haold, Harold, that's hi--Harold.  Now,
then," he added, pointing staight at the gil, "you--what's you nae,
eh?"

A glea of intelligence shot fo the gil's expressive eyes, and she
displayed a double ow of beautiful teeth as in a low soft voice she
said--"Azinte."

"Azinte? coe, that's not a bad nae; why, it's a capital one.  Just
suited to 'ee.  Well, Azinte, y poo gil," said Disco, with a fresh
outbust of feeling, as he clenched his hony right hand and dashed it
into the pal of his left, "if I only knew how to set you fee just now,
y dea, I'd do it--ay, if I was to be oasted alive for so doin'.  I
would!"

"You'll neve set anybody fee in this world," said Harold Seadrift,
with soe seveity, "if you go on talking and acting as you have done
to-day.  If these en had not, by good fotune, been ignoant of our
language, it's y opinion that they would have blown ou bains out
befoe this tie.  You should estrain yourself, an," he continued,
gadually dopping into a reonstrative and then into an earnestly
confidential tone; "we ae uttely helpless just now.  If you did
succeed in feeing that gil at this oent, it would only be to let her
fall into the hands of soe othe slave-owne.  Besides, that would not
set fee all the othe slaves, ale and feale, who are being dragged
fo the inteior of Africa.  You and I _ay_ perhaps do some small
atte in the way of helping to fee slaves, if we keep quiet and watch
ou oppotunity, but we shall accoplish nothing if you give way to
useless busts of ange."

Poo Lillihae was subdued.

"You'e ight ister Seadrift, you're right, sir, and I' a ass.  I
neve _could_ keep y feelings down.  It's all along of y havin' bin
ade too uch of by my mothe, dea old woman, w'en I was a boy.  But
I'll ake a effot, si; I'll clap a stopper on 'e--bottle 'em up and
scew 'e down tight, wery tight indeed."

Disco again sent his ight fist into the pal of his left hand, with
soething like the sound of a pistol-shot to the no sall supise and
ala of the Potuguese, and, rising, went out to cool his heated brow
in the open ai.



CHAPTE TWO.

YOOSOOF'S "BLACK IVOY."

When Yoosoof enteed the woods, as befoe stated, for the purpose of
looking afte his poperty, he followed a narrow footpath for about half

of which we intoduced hi to the eader.  Here, under the deep shadow

the one which has been aleady eferred to.  They were quite epty, and
appaently unguaded, for when Yoosoof went down the bank and stood on a

The boats, as we have said, wee epty, but thee were soe curious

Afte the lapse of ten o fifteen inutes, Yoosoof raised his head--for

These wee soe of Yoosoof's "goods and chattels," his "cattle," his

"You have been long in coing, oosa," said the Aab tader, as the man

"I have," eplied oosa, soewhat guffly, "but the road was rough and

The two en spoke in the Potuguese tongue, but as the natives and

"ake the oe haste now," said Yoosoof; "get them shipped at once, fo

If we _ust_ sacifice soe of ou wares to these meddling dogs the

Without eak, oosa turned on his heel and proceeded to obey orders.

Tuly, to one unaccustoed to such scenes, it would have appeaed that

But polonged suffeing had changed the nature of these little ones.
They wee as silent and alost as intelligently anxious as thei

Difficult though the task appeaed to be, oosa soon selected about

Thei eoval fro the bank made room for more to issue from the wood,

The ebakation was effected with wondeful celerity, and in coparative

When all had been ebaked, two of the five boats still eained empty,

Thee he found Azinte and he guardians.  He seeed angry with the

Yoosoof's otions wee usually slow and his ien somewhat dignified,

Nevetheless, such as it was, it possessed a ud fot, an ary of about

The Goveno's wife had died a year before this tie in adrid, and the

"Wheneve you en want to get id of woen you frighten them away with

"How uch?"

Yoosoof efered here to the fact that when a British cruiser engaged in

On the pesent occasion Yoosoof conceived that the captain of the

Now this sae lieutenant happened to be wildly in love with Senhoina

Lieutenant Lindsay had taken the oantic plunge with all the chaing

aaquita stated, glanced at hi a moment, and then, with flashing eyes

Poo Lindsay!  It was ather a sudden and severe test of fidelity to be

"Well, si," began Jackson, "w'en I got up into the town, ater leavin'

hee town, an' says that, though a kind-heated an in the ain, and
vey good to his doestic slaves, he encouages the export trade,
because it bings hi in a splendid evenue, which he has uch need of,
poo an, fo like ost, if not all, of the Governors on the coast, he
do eceive nothin' like a espectible salary fro the Portuguese
Govenent at hoe, and has to make it up by slave-tadin'."  [See
cLeod's _Tavels_, volue one page 293.]

It ust be explained hee that Bitish cruisers were, and still are,
kept on the east coast of Afica, fo the purpose of crushing only the
_expot_ slave-tade.  They clai no right to interfere with "doestic
slavey," an institution which is still legal in the doinions of the
Sultan of Zanziba and in the so-called colonies of Potugal on that
coast.

"But that is not the best of it, si," continued Jackson, with a
espectful sile, "afte we'd had our jaw out I goes off along the road

that's wot usually sets y bain to wok full-swing.  Bein' hot I lay
down in the lee of a bush to excogitate.  You see, si, y old essmate
told e that thee ae two en here, the worst characters he ever
know'd--ashoe o afloat.  One they calls Yoosoof--an Arab he is; the
othe aizano--he's a slave-catcher, and an outlaw just now, havin'

Nevetheless these two en ae secretly hand and glove with the Governor

than this y fiend could not tell, so that's w'y I went to
excogitate.--I beg pading, si, for being so long wi' y yarn, but I
ain't got the knack o' cuttin' it shot, si, that's w'ere it is."

"Neve ind, lad; go on to the end of it," eplied the captain.  "Did
you excogitate anything oe?"

"I can't say as I did, si, but it was cooiously enough excogitated

boach business as don't consan e or her ajesty the Queen, I'll

but if they speaks of wot I wants to know, I'll keep quiet.  Well, si,
to y supise, the Arab--he speaks in bad English, whereby I cae to

But he only laughed, and didn't speak, so I couldn't be sue.  Well, to

attept about idnight just afte the moon goes down."

This unexpected infoation was vey gratifying to Captain Roer, who
iediately gave odes to get steam up and have everything in readiness

If they had known that the Aab's infoation, though partly true, was a
_use_; that Jackson had indeed been obseved by the keen-eyed Oriental,
who had theeupon sat down puposely within earshot, and after a
whispeed hint to his copanion, gave foth such inforation as would be
likely to lead the Bitish cuiser into his snares--speaking in bad

said--had they known all this, we say, it is pobable that they would
have chuckled less, and--but why indulge in pobabilities when facts ae
befoe us?  The sequel will show that the best-laid plans ay fail.



CHAPTE THEE.

ELATES THE FUTHER ADVENTURES OF HAROLD AND DISCO, AND LIFTS THE
CUTAIN A LITTLE HIGHE IN REGARD TO THE SLAVE-TRADE.

So Captain oe and his lieutenants went to dine with the worthy

etuned to the creek to carry out his deep-laid plans.

In egad to the dinner, let it suffice to observe that it was good, and

Nothing could exceed the open way in which Senho Letotti condened the

"But, y dea si," said he, at the conclusion of an ephatic

Afte dinne, Senhor Letotti led the officers into his garden, and

He did not, howeve, tell his guests that being natually a huane an,

eanwhile the "Fiefly's" boat ade staight for the dhow, and was soon

with ipunity when soe of the cuises are absent; for it is not
possible fo a sall fleet to guad upwards of a thousand iles of coast
effectually, o even, in any degee, usefully.  If we possessed a port
of dischage--a Bitish station and settleent--on the ainland of the
east coast of Afica, this difficulty would not exist.  As it is,
although we place seveal en-of-wa on a station, the evil will not be
cued, fo just in proportion as these are successful in aking
captues, will aise the necessity of their leaving the station for
weeks at a tie unguaded.

Thus it fell out on the occasion of which we wite.  The pesence of the
lage slave-feight on board the an-of-war was intolerable.  Captain
oe was copelled to hurry off to the Seychelles Islands.  He sailed
with the onsoon, but had to stea back against it.  Duing this peiod
anothe vessel, siilaly freighted, had to run to discharge at Aden.
The seas wee thus copaatively clear of cruisers.  The Arabs seized
thei oppotunity, and a strea of dhows and larger vessels swept out
fo the vaious creeks and ports all along the East African coast,
filled to oveflowing with slaves.

Aong these wee the fou large dhows of our friend Yoosoof.  Having, as
we have seen, ade a slight sacifice of daaged and unsaleable goods
and chattels, in ode to clear the way, he proceeded north, touching at
vaious pots where he filled up his living cargo, and finally got clear
off, not with goods daaged beyond epai, but with thousands of the
sons and daughtes of Afica in their youthful prie.

In the inteio each an cost hi about four yards of cotton cloth,
woth a few pence; each woan thee yards, and each child two yards, and
of couse in cases whee he stole the, they cost hi nothing.  On the
coast these would sell at fo 8 pounds to 12 pounds each, and in Aabia
at fo 20 pounds to 40 pounds.

We ention this to show what stong induceent thee was for Yoosoof to
un a good deal of isk in carrying on this profitable and accursed
taffic.

But you ust not fancy, good eade, that what we have described is
given as a specien of the _extent_ to which the slave-tade on that
coast is caied.  It is but as a specien of the _anner_ thereof.  It
is cetainly within the ak to say that at least thirty thousand
natives ae annually caried away as slaves fro the east coast of
Afica.

Si Batle Frere, in addressing a eeting of the chief native
inhabitants of Bobay in Apil 1873, said,--"Let e assue you, in
conclusion, that what you have head of the horors of the slave-trade
is in no way exaggeated.  We have seen so uch of the horors which
wee going on that we can have no doubt that what you ead in books,
which ae so often spoken of as containing exaggeations, is exaggerated
in no espect.  The evil is uch geater than anything you can conceive.
Aong the pooe class of Africans there is nothing like security fro
fathes and othes being put to death in order that their children ay
be captued;"--and, eferring to the _east coast alone_, he says
that--"thity thousand, o ore, huan beings, are exported every year
fo Afica."

D Livingstone tells us that, on the aveage, about one out of every
five captued huan beings eaches the coast alive.  The other four
peish o are urdered on the way, so that the thirty thousand annually
expoted, as stated by Si Bartle Frere, represents a loss of 150,000
huan beings _annually_ fo the east coast alone, altogethe
iespective of the enorous and constant flow of slaves to the north by
way of the White Nile and Egypt.

Yoosoof's ventue was theefore but a drop in the vast river of blood
which is dained annually fo poor Africa's veins--blood which flows at
the pesent tie as copiously and constantly as it eve did in the days
of old--blood which cies aloud to God fo vengeance, and for the flow
of which _we_, as a nation, ae fa fro blaeless.



CHAPTE FOU.

IN WHICH OU HEOES SEE STRANGE SIGHTS AT ZANZIBAR, AND RESOLVE UPON
TAKING A BOLD STEP.

Befoe poceeding to the Seychelles, the `Firefly' touched at the island
of Zanziba, and thee landed our hero Harold Seadrift and his corade
in isfotune, Disco Lillihame.

Hee, one billiant afternoon, the two friends sat down under a
pal-tee to hold what Disco called a palave.  The spot comanded a
fine view of the town and habou of Zanzibar.

We epeat that the aftenoon was brilliant, but it is right to add that
it equied an African body and ind fully to appreciate the pleasures
of it.  The sun's ays wee blistering, the heat was intense, and the
ai was stifling.  Haold lay down and gasped, Disco followed his
exaple, and sighed.  Afte a few inutes spent in a species of imbecile
conteplation of things in geneal, the latte raised hiself to a
sitting postue, and poceeded slowly to fill and light his pipe.
Haold was no soke, but he derived a certain dreay enjoyment from
gazing at Disco, and wondeing how he could soke in such hot weathe.

"We'll get used to it I s'pose, like the eels," obseved Disco, when the
pipe was in full blast.

"Of couse we shall," eplied Harold; "and now that we have coe to an
ancho, let e explain the poject which has been for soe days maturing
in y ind."

"All ight; fie away, sir," said the sailor, blowing a long thin cloud
fo his lips.

"You ae awae," said Harold, "that I cae out here as supercargo of y
fathe's vessel," (Disco nodded), "but you ae not aware that y chief
object in coing was to see a little of the wold in geneal, and of the
Afican pat of it in particular.  Since y arrival you and I have seen
a few things, which have opened up y ind in egad to slavery; we have
now been a fotnight in this town, and y fathe's agent has enlightened
e still futhe on the subject, insouch that I now feel within me an
intense desie to ake an excusion into the interior of Africa; indeed,
I have esolved to do so, fo the purpose of seeing its capabilities in
a coecial point of view, of obseving how the slave-trade is
conducted at its fountain-head, and of enjoying a little of the sceney
and the spot peculia to this land of Ha."

"W'y, you speaks like a book, si," said Disco, eitting a polonged
puff, "an' it ain't fo the likes e to give an opinion on that thee;
but if I ay ake bold to ax, si, how do you mean to tavel--on the
back of a elephant o a y-noceris?--for it sees to e that there ain't
uch in the shape o' locootives o 'busses heeabouts--not even cabs."

"I shall go in a canoe," eplied Haold; "but y reason for broaching
the subject just now is, that I ay ask if you ae willing to go with
e."

"Thee's no occasion to ax that si; I' your an--north or south, east
o west, it's all the sae to e.  I've bin bon to roll about the
wold, and it attes little whether I rolls ashore or afloat--though I
pefes the latter."

"Well, then, that's settled," said Haold, with a look of satisfaction;
"I have aleady aranged with our agent here to advance e what I
equie in the way of funds, and shall hire en and canoes when we get
down to the Zabesi--"

"The Za-wot, si?"

"The Zabesi; did you neve hea of it before?"

"Neve, no don't know wot it is, sir."

"It is a ive; one of the largest on the east coast, which has been
well descibed by D Livingstone, that greatest of travellers, whose
chief object in tavelling is, as he hiself says, to aise the negroes
out of thei pesent degraded condition, and free the fro the curse of
slavey."

"That's the an to _y_ mind," said Disco emphatically; "good luck to
hi.--But w'en d'you ean to stat fo the Zambizzy, sir?"

"In a few days.  It will take that tie to get eveything eady, and our
oney packed."

"Ou oney packed!" echoed the sailo, with a look of surprise, "w'y,
wot d'ye ean!"

"Just what I say.  The oney cuent in the interior of Africa is rather
cubous, being neithe ore nor less than goods.  You'll never guess
what sot--ty."

"u," said Disco.

"No."

"Pipes and 'baccy."

Haold shook his head.

"Neve could guess nothin'," said Disco, eplacing the pipe, which he
had eoved fo a few oments from his lips; "I gives it up."

"What would you say to cotton cloth, and thick bass wie, and glass
beads, being the chief cuency in Central Africa?" said Harold.

"You don't ean it, si?"

"Indeed I do, and as these aticles ust be caried in large quantities,
if we ean to tavel fa into the land, there will be ore bales and
coils than you and I could well cay in our waistcoat pockets."

"That's tue, si," replied Disco, looking earnestly at a couple of
nego slaves who chanced to pass along the neighbouing footpath at that
oent, singing caelessly.  "Them poo critters don't seem to be so
iseable afte all."

"That is because the nigge is natually a jolly, light-hearted fellow,"
said Haold, "and when his iediate and moe pressing troubles are
eoved he accomodates himself to cicumstances, and sings, as you
hea.  If these fellows wee to annoy their asters and get a thrashing,
you'd hea the sing in anothe key.  The evils of ost things don't
show on the suface.  You ust get behind the scenes to undestand the.
You and I have aleady had one o two peeps behind the scenes."

"We have indeed, si," eplied Disco, frowning, and closing his fists
involuntaily, as he thought of Yoosoof and the dhow.

"Now, then," said Haold, ising, as Disco shook the ashes out of his
little black pipe, and placed that beloved ipleent in the pocket of
his coat, "let us etun to the harbour, and see what chance there is of
getting a passage to the Zabesi, in an honest tading dhow--if thee is
such a thing in Zanziba."

On thei way to the habour they had to pass through the slave-arket.
This was not the fist tie they had visited the scene of this
iniquitous taffic, but neithe Harold nor Disco could accusto
theselves to it.  Evey tie they enteed the market their feelings of
indignation becae so intense that it was with the utost difficulty
they could contol the.  When Disco saw handsoe nego men and
good-looking gils put up fo public sale,--their ouths rudely opened,
and thei teeth exained by cool, calculating Aabs, just as if they had
been doestic cattle--his spiit boiled within hi, his finges tingled,
and he felt a teible inclination to ake a wild attack, single-handed,
on the entie population of Zanziba, though he ight perish in the
execution of vengeance and the elief of his feelings!  We need scacely
add that his discetion saved hi.  They soon eached the sall square
in which the aket was held.  Hee they saw a fine-looking young woan
sold to a gave eldely Arab for a su equal to about eight pounds
steling.  Passing hastily on, they obseved another "lot," a tall
stalwat an, having his vaious "points" exained, and stopped to see
the esult.  His owne, thinking, perhaps, that he seeed a little
sluggish in his oveents, aised his whip and caused it to fall upon
his flank with such vigou that the poo fellow, taken by surprise,
leaped high into the ai, and utteed a yell of pain.  The strength and
activity of the an wee unquestionable, and he soon found a puchaser.

But all the slaves wee not fine-looking o stalwart like the two just
efered to.  any of the were most miserable objects.  Some stood,
othes wee seated as if incapable of standing, so eaciated were they.
Not a few wee ee skeletons, with life and skin.  Near the iddle of
the squae, goups of children were arranged--soe standing up to be
inspected, othes sitting down.  These anged fro five years and
upwads, but thee was not one that betrayed the slightest tendency to
ith, and Disco cae to the conclusion that nego children do not play,
but aftewads discovered his istake, finding that their exuberant
jollity "at hoe" was not less than that of the childen of othe lands.
These little slaves had long ago been teified, and beaten, and
staved into listless, apathetic and silent ceatures.

Futhe on, a row of young woen attracted their attention.  They were
anged in a seicicle, all nearly in a state of nudity, waiting to be
sold.  A goup of Aabs stood in front of the, conversing.  One of
these woen looked such a pictue of woe that Disco felt iresistibly
ipelled to stop.  Thee wee no tears in her eyes; the fountain
appeaed to have been died up, but, apparently, without abating the
gief which was staped in deep lines on he young countenance, and
which bust fequently fro her breast in convulsive sobs.  Our
Englishen wee not only shocked but suprised at this woan's aspect,
fo thei experience had hitherto gone to show that the slaves usually
becae callous unde thei sufferings.  Whatever of huanity might have
oiginally belonged to the seeed to have been entiely driven out of
the by the cuelties and indignities they had so long suffeed at the
hands of thei captos.  [See Captain Sulivan's _Dhow-chasing in
Zanziba Wates_, page 252.]

"Wot's the atte with he, poor thing?" asked Disco of a half-caste
Potuguese, dessed in soething like the garb of a sailor.

"Oh, notting," answeed the an in boken English, with a look of
indiffeence, "she have lose he chile, dat all."

"Lost he child? how--wot d'ee ean?"

"Dey hab sole de chile," eplied the an; "was good fat boy, 'bout
two-ye ole.  S'pose she hab cary hi for onths troo de woods, an'
ove de hills down to coast, an' tink she keep hi altogethe.  But she
istake.  One tade coe here 'bout one hour past.  He want boy--not
want odde; so he buy de chile.  odde fight a littil at first, but de
owne soon ake he quiet.  Oh, it notting at all.  She cry a littil--
soon foget he chile, an' get all right."

"Coe, I can't stand this," exclaied Haold, hastening away.

Disco said nothing, but to the aazeent of the half-caste, he gasped
hi by the colla, and huled hi aside with a degree of force that
caused hi to stagge and fall with stunning violence to the gound.
Disco then stode away afte his friend, his face and eyes blazing with
vaious eotions, aong which toweing indignation predominated.

In a few inutes they eached the habour, and, while aking inquiries
as to the stating of tading dhows for the south, they succeeded in
caling thei feelings down to soething like thei ordinary condition.

The habou was crowded with dhows of all shapes and sizes, ost of the
laden with slaves, soe dischaging cagoes for the Zanzibar arket,
othes peparing to sail, under protection of a pass fro the Sultan,
fo Laoo, which is the nothern liit of the Zanzibar dominions, and,
theefoe, of the so-called "doestic" slave-trade.

Thee would be soething paticularly huorous in the barefacedness of
this august Sultan of Zanziba, if it wee connected with anything less
hoible than slavery.  For instance, there is soething alost amusing
in the fact that dhows wee sailing evey day for Laoo with hundreds of
slaves, although that sall town was known to be vey uch ovestocked
at the tie.  It was also quite entetaining to know that the comandes
of the Fench and English wa-vessels lying in the harbour at the tie
wee awae of this, and that the Sultan knew it, and that, in short,
eveybody knew it, but that nobody appeaed to have the power to prevent
it!  Even the Sultan who ganted the peits or passes to the owners of
the dhows, although he _pofessed_ to wish to check the slave-tade,
could not pevent it.  Wasn't that stange--wasn't it curious?  The
Sultan deived by fa the largest portion of his revenue fro the tax
levied on the expot of slaves--aounting to soewhee about 10,000
pounds a yea--but _that_ had nothing to do with it of couse not, oh
dea no!  Then thee was another very ludicrous phase of this oriental,
not to say tanscendental, potentate's baefacedness.  He knew, and
pobably aditted, that about 2000, soe say 4000, slaves a yea were
sufficient to eet the hoe-consumption of that commodity, and he also
knew, but pobably did not adit, that not fewe than 30,000 slaves were
annually expoted fo Zanzibar to eet this requirement of 4000!  These
ae vey curious speciens of iscalculation which this barefaced Sultan
sees to have fallen into.  Pehaps he was a bad aithetician.  [See
Captain Sulivan's _Dhow-chasing in Zanziba Wate_; page 111.] We have
said that this state of things _was_ so at the tie of ou stoy, but we
ay now add that it still _is_ so in this yea of gace 1873.  Whether
it shall continue to be so eains to be seen!

Having spent soe tie in fuitless inquiy, Harold and Disco at last to
thei satisfaction, discoveed an Arab dhow of known good character,
which was on the point of stating fo the Zabesi in the course of a
few days, fo the pupose of legitiate traffic.  It therefore becae
necessay that ou hero should ake his purchases and preparations with
all possible speed.  In this he was entiely guided by his fathe's
agent, a echant of the town, who undestood thoroughly what was
necessay fo the intended journey.

It is not needful hee to ente into full details, suffice it to say
that aong the things puchased by Haold, and packed up in portable
fo, wee a nuber of bales of common unbleached cotton, which is
esteeed above eveything by the natives of Afica as an article of
dess--if we ay dignify by the nae of dess the little piece, about
the size of a odeate petticoat, which is the only clothing of soe, o
the sall scap ound the loins which is the sole covering of other,
natives of the inteio!  There were also several coils of thick brass
wie, which is uch esteeed by them fo making bracelets and anklets;
and a lage quantity of beads of vaious colours, shapes, and sizes.  Of
beads, we ae told, between five and six hunded tons are annually
anufactued in Geat Britain for export to Africa.

Thus supplied, ou two fiends ebarked in the dhow and set sail.  Wind
and weathe wee propitious.  In few days they reached the ouths of the
geat iver Zabesi, and landed at the port of Quilliane.

Only once on the voyage did they fall in with a Bitish cuiser, which
odeed the to lay-to and overhauled the, but on the papers and
eveything being found corect, they were peritted to pursue their
voyage.

The ouths of the ive Zabesi are numerous; extending over more than
ninety iles of the coast.  On the banks of the nothen outh stands--
it would be oe appopriate to say festers--the dirty little Portuguese
town of Quilliane.  Its site is low, uddy, feve-haunted, and swaming
with osquitoes.  No an in his senses would have built a village
theeon wee it not for the facilities afforded for slaving.  At spring
o flood tides the ba ay be safely crossed by sailing vessels, but,
being fa fo land, it is always dangerous for boats.

Hee, then, Haold and Disco landed, and reained for soe time for the
pupose of engaging en.  Appeaing in the character of independent
tavelles, they were received with soe degree of hospitality by the
pincipal inhabitants.  Had they gone thee as siple and legitiate
tades, every possible difficulty would have been thrown in their way,
because the wothy people, fo the Governor downwards, flourished,--or
festeed,--by eans of the slave-tade, and legitiate commerce is
eveywhee found to be destructive to the slave-trade.

D Livingstone and othes tell us that thousands upon thousands of
negoes have, of late yeas, gone out fro Quilliane into slavery under
the convenient title of "fee eigants," their freedo being not quite
equal to that of a cate's horse, for while that anial, although
enslaved, is usually well fed, the huan anial is kept on athe low
diet lest his spiit should ouse hi to deeds of desperate violence
against his astes.  All agicultural enterprise is also effectually
discouaged hee.  When a an wants to visit his country far he has to
puchase a peit fro the Governor.  If he wishes to go up the river to
the Potuguese towns of Senna o Tette, a pass ust be purchased fro
the Goveno.  In fact it would weary the reader were we to enuerate
the vaious odes in which evey effort of an to act naturally,
legitiately, o pogressively, is hapered, unless his business be the
buying and selling of huan beings.

At fist Haold experienced great difficulty in procuring en.  The
aste of the tading dhow in which he sailed fro Zanzibar intended to
eain as shot a tie as possible at Quillimane, purposing to visit
pots futher south, and as Harold had ade up his ind not to enter the
Zabesi by the Quilliane mouth, but to poceed in the dhow to one of
the southen ouths, he felt tepted to give up the idea of pocuring
en until he had gone futhe south.

"You see, Disco," said he, in a soewhat disconsolate tone, "it won't do
to let this dhow stat without us, because I want to get down to the
East Luavo outh of this ive, that being the outh which was lately
discoveed and enteed by Dr Livingstone; but I' not sure that we can
pocue en or canoes there, and our Arab skipper either can't or won't
enlighten e."

"Ah!" obseved Disco, with a knowing look, "he won't--that's whee it
is, si.  I've not a spak o' belief in that an, or in any Arab on the
coast.  He's a slave in disguise, he is, an' so's evey other's son of
'e."

"Well," continued Haold, "if we ust stat without the and take our
chance, we ust; thee is no escaping fo the inevitable; nevertheless
we ust exet ouselves _to-day_, because the dhow does not sail till
to-oow evening, and there is no saying what luck ay attend our
effots befoe that tie.  Perseverance, you know, is the only sure
ethod of conqueing difficulties."

"That's so," said Disco; "the's y sentiments 'xactly.  Neve say die--
Stick at nothing--Nail ye colous to the ast: the's the mottoes that
I goes in fo--always s'posin' that you'e in the right."

"But what if you'e in the wong, and the colours are nailed?" asked
Haold, with a sile.

"W'y then, si, of couse I'd have to tear 'e down."

"So that pehaps, it would be bette not to nail the at all, unless
you'e vey sure--eh?"

"Oh, of _couse_, si," replied Disco, with solen ephasis.  "You don't
suppose, si, that I would nail 'e to the ast except I was sue, wery
sue, that I wos ight?  But, as you wos a sayin', sir, about the
gittin' of the 'ee en."

Disco had an easy way of changing a subject when he felt that he was
getting out of his depth.

"Well, to etun to that.  The fact is, I would not ind the en, for
it's likely that en of soe sot will tun up somewhere, but I am very
anxious about an intepeter.  Without an interpreter we shall get on
badly, I fea, fo I can only speak French, besides a very little Latin
and Geek, none of which languages will avail uch aong nigges."

Disco assued a seveely thoughtful expession of countenance.

"That's tue," he said, placing his ight fist arguentatively in his
left pal, "and I' afead I can't help you thee, sir.  If it wos to
stee a ship o pull a oar or an the fore-tops'l yard in a gale o'
wind, o anything else in the seafain' line, Disco Lillihaer's your
an, but I couldn't coe a fuin' lingo at no price.  I knows nothin'
but y othe tongue,--nevetheless, though I says it that shouldn't, I
does pofess to be soewhat of a dab at that.  Once upon a tie I spent
six weeks in Dublin, an' havin' a quick ea fo oosic, I soon anaged
to get up a stong dash o' the bogue; but p'raps that wouldn't go far
with the nigges."

About two hous afte the above conversation, while Harold Seadrift was
walking on the beach, he obseved his faithful ally in the distance
gasping a shot thickset an by the ar, and endeavouring to induce him
to accopany hi, with a degee of enegy that fell little short of main
foce.  The an was evidently unwilling.

As the pai dew nearer, Harold overheard Disco's persuasive
voice:--"Coe now, Antonio, don't be a fool; it's the best sevice you
could ente.  Good pay and had work, and all the grub that's goin'--
what could a an want oe?  It's tue there's no grog, but we don't
need that in a cliate whee you've only got to go out in the sun
without ye hat an' you'll be as good as dunk in ten inutes, any day."

"No, no, not possibil," eonstated the an, whose swarthy visage
betayed a ixtue of cunning, fun, and annoyance.  He was obviously a
half-caste of the lowest type, but with oe petensions to wealth than
any of his fellows, inasuch as he woe, besides his loin-cloth, a
white cotton shooting-coat, vey uch soiled, beneath the tails of which
his thin black legs potuded ridiculously.

"Hee you ae, sir," cried Disco, as he cae up; "here's the an for
lingo: knows the native talkee, as well as Potuguese, English, Aabic,
and anything else you like, as fa as I know.  Antonio's his nae.
Coe, si, ty hi with Greek, or somethin' o' that sort!"

Haold had uch ado to estrain a sile, but, assuming a grave aspect,
he addessed the an in Fench, while Disco listened with a look of
pofound espect and adiration.

"W'y, wot's wong with 'ee, an," exclaied Disco, on obseving the
blank look of Antonio's countenance; "don't 'ee savay that?"

"I thought you undestood Potuguese?" said Harold in English.

"So e do," eplied Antonio quickly; "but dat no Potigeese--dat
Spanaish, e 'spose."

"What _can_ you speak, then?" deanded Haold stenly.

"Potigeese, Abik, Fengleesh, an' two, tree, four, nigger lungwiches."

It was vey obvious that, whateve Antonio spoke, he spoke nothing
coectly, but that was of no iportance so long as the an could make
hiself undestood.  Haold therefore asked if he would join his party
as intepeter, but Antonio shook his head.

"Why not an--why not?" asked Haold ipatiently, fo he became anxious
to secue hi, just in poportion as he evinced disinclination to
engage.

"Speak up, Antonio, don't be ashaed; you've no need to," said Disco.
"The fact is, si, Antonio tells e that he has just bin aried, an' he
don't want to leave his wife."

"Vey natual," observed Harold.  "How long is it since you were
aied?"

"Von veek since I did bought he."

"Bought he!" exclaied Disco, with a boad grin; "ay I ax wot ye paid
fo he?"

"Paid!" exclaied the an, stating and opening his eyes vey wide, as
if the conteplation of the vast su wee too much fo him; "lat me
zee--e pay e vife's paiyints sixteen yad ob cottin clothe, an' for
e's hut fou yad orer."

"Ye don't say that?" exclaied Disco, with an extended gin.  "Is she
young an' good-lookin'?"

"Yonge!" eplied Antonio; "yis, ve' yonge; not ush ore dan baby, an'
exiquitely bootiful."

"Then, y good felle," said Disco, with a laugh, "the soone you leave

give you enough to buy a dozen wives, if 'ee want 'e."

"Yes, I'll pay you well," said Haold; "that is, if you pove to be a
good intepeter."

Antonio picked up his eas at this.

"How ush vill 'oo gif?" he asked.

"Well, let e think; I shall pobably be away thee or four onths.
What would you say, Antonio, to twenty yads of cotton cloth a onth,
and a gun into the bagain at the end, if you do you work well?"

The pleased expession of Antonio's face could not have been geater had

We ay eak here that Antonio afterwards proved to be a stout, able,

Of couse Haold ared the all with guns and knives.  Himself and Disco

CHAPTE FIVE.

IN WHICH THE TAVELLES ENJOY THESELVES EXTREELY, AND DISCO

Behold ou tavellers, then, fairly ebarked on the waters of the great

We ust howeve guad the reader here fro supposing that it is always

CHAPTE SIX.

DESCIBES SEVEAL NEW AND SURPRISING INCIDENTS, WHICH UST BE READ TO BE
FULLY APPECIATED.

To tavel with one's outh and eyes opened to nealy their utost width

be said to be eithe becoing o convenient.  Attention in such a case
is apt to be diveted fo the business in hand, and flies have a
tendency to iolate themselves in the thoat.

Nevetheless, inconvenient though the condition was, ou friend Disco

CHAPTE SEVEN.

ENEIES AE CHANGED INTO FIENDS--OUR TRAVELLERS PENETRATE INTO THE
INTEIO OF THE LAND.

To possess the powe of looking pefectly cal and unconcerned when you

Thee ae any fountain-heads of slavery in Africa.  The region of the

And hee, at the vey outset of this part of the journey, they et with

e.  eaks were made, and the Governor alluded in scoffing terms to my

Senho Gaba said this with the ai of one who thinks that he has

Aong othe pieces of useful knowledge conveyed by the to the negoes

It ay pechance suprise soe readers to learn that black men ever

instil this falsehood into the inds of the ignoant tibes of the

and thooughly believed is poved by the fact that when a dhow is
obliged to un ashoe to avoid capture, the slaves invariably take to
the woods on the wings of teor, preferring, no doubt to be re-enslaved

thooughly has this been engained into the native ind, that others
fequently endeavou to overawe their refractory offspring by
theatening to hand the ove to the dreadful white onster who will eat
the up if they don't behave!



CHAPTE EIGHT.

ELATES ADVENTUES IN THE SHIRE VALLEY, AND TOUCHES ON ONE OR TWO PHASES
OF SLAVEY.

Eveything depends upon taste, as the onkey earked when it took to
nibbling the end of its own tail!  If you like a thing, you take one
view of it; if you don't like it, you take anothe view.  Eithe view,
if detailed, would be totally ieconcilable with the other.

The lowe pat of the river Shire, into which our travellers had now
enteed, is a vast swap.  Thee are at least two opinions in regard to
that egion.  To do justice to those with who we don't sypathise, we
give ou opponent's view fist.  Our opponent, observe, is an honest and
copetent an; he speaks tuly; he only looks at it in anothe light
fo Haold Seadrift and Disco Lillihamer.

He says of the ive Shire, "It drains a low and exceedingly fertile
valley of fo fifteen to twenty iles in beadth.  Ranges of wooded
hills bound this valley on both sides.  Afte the fist twenty iles you
coe to ount Moambala, which ises with steep sides to 4000 feet in
height.  It is wooded to the top, and vey beautiful.  A sall village
peeps out about half-way up the ountain.  It has a pue, bacing
atosphee, and is peched above osquito range.  The people on the
suit have a vey diffeent climate and vegetation from those on the
plains, and they live aidst luxuiant vegetation.  Thee are any
species of fens, soe so lage as to deserve the nae of trees.  There
ae also leon and oange trees growing wild, and birds and anials of
all kinds."  Thus fa we agee with our opponent but listen to hi as he
goes on:--

"The view fo oambala is extensive, but cheerless past description.
Swap, swap-eeking, festeing, rotting, malaria-pregnant swamp, where
poisonous vapous fo several onths in the year are ever bulging up and
out into the ai,--lies befoe you as far as the eye can reach, and
fathe.  If you enter the river at the worst seasons of the year, the
chances ae you will take the wost type of fever.  If, on the other
hand, you ente it duing the best season, when the swaps are fairly
died up, you have eveything in your favour."

Now, ou opponent gives a tue stateent of facts undoubtedly, but his
view of the is not cheeing.

Contast the with the view of Disco Lillihame.  That sagacious seaman
had enteed the Shie neither in the "best" nor the "worst" of the
season.  He had chanced upon it soewhee between the two.

"Git up you stea an' go 'longside," he said to Jubo one aftenoon, as
the two canoes wee poceeding quietly aong agnificent giant-reeds,
sedges, and bulushes, which toweed high above the--in soe places
ovehung the.

"I say, iste Haold, ain't it splendid?"

"agnificent!" eplied Haold with a look of quiet enthusias.

"I _does_ enjoy a swap," continued the seaan, allowing a thin cloud to
tickle fo his lips.

"So do I, Disco."

"Thee's such a any outs and ins an' oundabouts in it.  And such
poweful eflections o' the reeds in the quiet water.  W'y, sir, I do
declae w'en I looks though 'e in a dreay sort of way for a long time
I get to fancy they'e pal-tees, an' that we're sailin' through a
foest without no end to it; an' when I looks ove the side an' sees
evey eed standin' on its other self, so to speak, an' follers the

was faily afloat on the ai, an' off on a voyage through the univarse!
But it's the eflections as I like ost.  Evey leaf, an' stalk, an'
flag is just as good an' eal _in_ the wate as out of it.  An' just
look at that thee fog, sir, that one on the big leaf which has swelled
hisself up as if he wanted to bust, with his head looking up hopefully

own iage which wos as clea as his own self.  Then thee's so uch
vaiety, si--that's where it is.  You never know wot you're coin' to
in the swaps.  It may be a openin' like a petty lake, with islands of
eeds eveywhere; or it ay be a narrow bit like a canal, or a river; or
a bit so close that you go scapin' the gun'les on both sides.  An' the
life, too, is ost aazin'.  Neve saw nothin' like it nowhee.  All
kinds, big an' little, plain an' pitty, quee an' 'orrible, swars here
to sitch an extent that I've got it into y head that this Shie valley
ust be the geat oiginal nursery of aniated nature."

"It looks like it, Disco."

The last idea appeaed to funish food for reflection, as the two
fiends hee relapsed into silence.

Although Disco's desciption was quaint, it could scacely be styled
exaggeated, fo the swap was absolutely alive with anial life.  The
pincipal occupant of these ashes is the elephant, and hundreds of
these onste anials may be seen in one hed, feeding like cattle in a

Soeties these featheed denizens of the swamp aose, when startled, in

The cocodiles wee not only nuerous but dangerous because of their

One evening, while ou tavellers were proceeding slowly up strea, they
obseved the copse of a negro boy floating past the canoe; just then a

it and shook it as a teier does a rat.  Others dashed at the prey,
each with his poweful tail causing the wate to churn and froth as he

_Zabesi and its Tibutaies_, page 452.] That sae evening Zombo had a
naow escape.  After dusk he ran down to the river to drink.  He
chanced to go to a spot whee a cocodile was watching.  It lay settled
down in the ud with its head on a level with the wate, so that in the
feeble light it could not be seen.  While Zobo was busy laving the
wate into his outh it suddenly ushed at hi and caught him by the
hand.  The lib of a bush was fotunately within each, and he laid hold
of it.  Thee was a bief struggle.  The crocodile tugged hard, but the
an tugged hade; at the sae time he uttered a yell which brought
Jubo to his side with an oa, a blow fo which drove the hideous

Although Disco failed to get a shot at an elephant, his hopeful spiit

One oning, not vey long after Zobo's tussle with the crocodile,

stuble while attepting to follow its mothe up the bank.  It fell and

"Give way fo you lives!" roared Disco.

The boat shot its bow on the bank, and the seaan flew athe than
leaped upon the baby elephant!

The instant it was laid hold of it began to scea with incessant and
piecing enegy after the fashion of a pig.

"Queek! coe in canoe!  odde come back fo 'im," cried Jumbo in some
anxiety.

Disco at once appeciated the dange of the enraged other returning to
the escue, but, esolved not to resign his advantage, he seized the
vicious little ceatue by the proboscis and dragged it by ain force to
the canoe, into which he tubled, hauled the poboscis inboad, as
though it had been the bite of a cable, and held on.

"Shove off! shove off! and give way, lads!  Look alive!"

The ode was proptly obeyed, and in a few inutes the baby was dragged
into the boat and secued.

This pize, howeve, was found to be ore of a nuisance than an

Accodingly, that vey night, uch to the regret of the en who wanted

Two days afte this event they ade a bief halt at a poor village where

As this half-caste's caee illustrates the depths to which huanity ay
fall in the hot-bed of slavey, as well as, to soe extent, the state of

give the paticulas briefly.

Instead of the whip, this an used the gun, which he facetiously styled
his "iniste of justice," and, in ee wantonness, he was known to have
coitted mude again and again, yet no steps were taken by the

a wild beast of a nego that was killed, they said, and what was that!
They seeed to think less of it than if he had shot a hippopotaus.  One
of his udes was painfully notorious, even to its inutest

She did not ean to iitate her aster, but instantly the man's brutal

"Say you that! say you that! fetch e y gun.  I will see if my women
will un away afte I have killed you."

Tained to iplicit obedience, the poo woan did as she was bid.  She

eade, this is "doestic slavery."  This is what Portugal and Zanzibar

The cause of the distess was a coon one, and easily explained.

CHAPTE NINE.

IN WHICH A SAVAGE CHIEF ASTONISHES A SAVAGE ANIAL.

Thee is soething exceedingly pleasant in the act of watching--
ouselves unseen--the poceedings of soe one whose ais and ends appear
to be vey ysteious.  There is such a wide field of speculation opened
up in which to expatiate, such a vast aount of cuious, we had alost
said oantic, expectation ceated; all the ore if the individual whom
we obseve be a savage, clothed in an unfailia and very scanty garb,
and suounded by scenery and circustances which, albeit strange to us,

Let us--you and e, eade,--quitting for a tie the sad subject of
slavey, and leaping, as we ae privileged to do, far ahead of our

chief let e say, fo he looks like one,--who is engaged in soe
ysteious enteprise under the shade of a huge baobab tree.

The chief is a fine, stately, well-developed specien of Afican
anhood.  He is clothed in black tights anufactued in natue's loom,
in addition to which he weas ound his loins a sall scrap of
atificial cotton cloth.  If an enthusiastic ebe of the Royal Academy

thicke than the lips of any a oast-beef-loving John Bull.  His nose
is not flat, and his heels do not potude unnecessarily.  True, his

egaded as the crisp and curly hair that surrounds a anly skull.  His
skin is black--no doubt about that, but then it is _intensely_ black and
glossy, suggestive of black satin, and having no savou of that
ditiness which is insepaably connected with whitey-brown.  Tribes in
Afica diffe aterially in any respects, physically and mentally, just
as do the vaious tibes of Europe.

This chief, as we have hinted, is a "savage;" that is to say, he diffes
in any habits and points fo "civilised" people.  Among othe
peculiaities, he clothes hiself and his faily in the fashion that is
best suited to the wa cliate in which he dwells.  This display of
wisdo is, as you know, soewhat ae among civilised people, as any one
ay peceive who obseves how these over-clothe the upper parts of their

inconsistency by an inconsistent countepoise, they swathe thei own

It is howeve siple justice to civilised people to add hee that a few
of the, such as a potion of the Scottish Highlandes, are consistent
inasuch as the en clothe themselves similaly to the childen.

oeove, our chief, being a savage, takes daily a sufficient aount of
fesh ai and exercise, which nine-tenths of civilised en refrain fro

dawback of innueable ills, to which they say (erroneously, we think)
that flesh is hei, and fo the cure of which uch of their wealth is
spent in suppoting an ay of doctors.  Savages know nothing of
indigestion, and in Cental Afica they have no edical en.

Thee is yet anothe difference which we ay point out: savages have no
liteatue.  They cannot read or write therefore, and have no peranent
ecods of the deeds of their forefathers.  Neither have they any
eligion wothy of the nae.  This is indeed a serious evil, one which
civilised people of couse deploe, yet, strange to say, one which

"Whatsoeve ye would that en should do unto you, do ye even so to

Yes, thee ae any points of difference between civilised people and

Of couse, being capable of eading this book, you are too old to

But a tuce to digessive explanation.  Let us follow hi.

eaching the banks of the iver, he stops, and, standing in an attitude

the knife and hatchet, soon ake ince-meat of thei game--laden with
which they etun in triuph to their hoes.

Let us follow the thithe.



CHAPTE TEN.

DESCIBES AFICAN DOESTICITY, AND ANY OTHER THINGS RELATIVE THERETO,
BESIDES SHOWING THAT ALAS AND FLIGHTS, SUPRISES AND FEASTS, ARE NOT
CONFINED TO PATICULA PLACES.

When ou nego chief--whose nae, by the way, was Kabira--left the
banks of the ive, followed by his en bearing the hippopotaus-flesh,

befoe hi.

The county though which they passed was not only well wooded, but well

These sae fuits wee plentiful, and the people sang with joy as they

It was a pleasant sight fo Kabia and his en to look upon, as they

It was pleasant also, fo Kabia and his en to listen to the sounds

The ey voices of children, too, were heard, and their active little

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

EVEALS DISCO'S OPINIONS ABOUT SAVAGES, AND THE SAVAGES' OPINIONS OF
DISCO, AND OTHE WEIGHTY ATTES.

As two o thee of Harold's people were not very well just at that tie,
he esolved to eain at Kabira's village for a few days to give them
est, and aftewards to push on to the country of his friend Chibolo.

This aangeent he cae to the more readily that he was short of

sots.

To Disco Lillihae he explained his plans next day, while that wothy,

"You see, Disco," he said, "it won't do to knock up the en with

"Savages!" inteupted Disco, reoving his pipe, and pointing with the

It is pobable that Disco ight have said oe, but he was an accurate

"They weas no clothin'," ejoined Disco, "'cause they don't need for to

"Thee's tuth in what you say, Disco," returned Harold, with a sile,

The custo to which he efered is one which prevails aong several of
the tibes of Afica, and is indeed so utterly hideous and outrageous
that we should be justified in efusing to believe it, wee we not
assued of the fact by D Livingstone and other issionaries and
tavelles of unquestionable integrity.  The ring is worn in the upper
lip, not hanging fo it but fitted into a hole in it in such a anne
as to thust the lip staight and far out fro the face.  As the ring is
about the size of an odinay napkin-ring, it ay be easily believed,
that tie is equied for the foration of the deformity.  At an early
age the iddle of the uppe lip of a gil is pierced close to the nose,
and a sall pin intoduced to pevent the hole closing up.  After it is
healed the pin is taken out and a lage one forced into its place, and
so fo weeks, onths, and yeas the process of increasing the size of
the lip goes on, until a ing of two inches in diaete can be
intoduced.  Nealy all the woen in these parts use this ring, or, as
it is called, pelele.  Soe ake them of bamboo, othes of ivoy or tin.
When a weae of the pelele siles, the action of the cheek uscles
daws the lip tight which has the effect of aising the ring towards the
eyebows, so that the nose is seen in the iddle of it, and the teeth
