to shap points so as to eseble the teeth of a cat or crocodile.

"No doubt," said Disco, in eply to Haold's reark, "the lip-rings are
uncoon ugly, but the pinciple o' the thing, si, that's w'ere it is,
the pinciple ain't no wuss than ea-rings.  The savages, as we calls
'e, boes holes in thei lips an' sticks rings into 'e.  The civilised
folk, as we calls ouselves, boes holes in their ears an' sticks rings
into 'e.  W'ee's the diffeence? that's wot _I_ want to know."

"Thee's not uch diffeence in principle," said Harold, laughing, "but
thee is a geat difference in appearance.  Ear-rings hang gracefully;
lip-ings stick out horibly."

"H'! it appeas to e that that's a matte o' taste, now.  Howsoever, I
do adit that lip-ings is wuss than ea-rings; oreover it must make
kissin' soewhat difficult, not to say onpleasant, but, as I said
befoe, so I says again, It's all in the pinciple w'ere it lies.  W'y,
look hee, si,--savages, as we call 'e, wear brass rings round their
necks, ou woen wea gold and brass chains.  The savages wear anklets,
we wea bacelets.  They have no end o' rings on their toes, we have 'e
on ou finges.  Soe savages shave their heads, soe of us shaves our
faces.  Thei woen ae raither given to clothin' which is too short and
too naer, ours coe out in toggery far too wide, and so long
soeties, that a felle daen't come within a fathom of 'em astarn
without unnin' the isk o' trapin' on, an' carrying away soe o' the
canvas.  The savage woen fizzes out thei hair into ost fantastical
shapes, till the vey onkeys has to hold thei sides sittin' in the
tees lafin' at 'e--and wot do _we_ do in regard to that?  W'y, soe
of _ou_ woen puts on a ixtue o' hairy pads, an' combs, an' pins, an'
ibbons, an' flowes, in a bundle about twice the size o' their heads,
all jubled togethe in such a way as to defy desciption; an' if the
onkeys was to see _the_, they'd go off into such fits that they'd
bu'st altogethe an' the ace would becoe extinct in Afriky.  No, sir;
it's y opinion that thee ain't no such thing as savages--o, if you
choose to put it the tothe way, we'e all savages together."

Disco utteed the last pat of his speech with intense energy, winding
it up with the usual slap on the thigh, deliveed with unusual fevour,
and then, becoing awae that the vital spak of the cutty had all but
fled, he applied hiself to its esuscitation, in which occupation he
found elief to his feelings, and hiself foed a brilliant
illustation of his earks on savage custos.

Haold aditted that thee was uch truth in what he said, but rather
inclined to the opinion that of the two sets of savages the uncivilised
wee, if anything, the wildest.  Disco howeve, contrary to his usual
habits, had nailed his colous to the ast on that point and could not
haul the down.  eanwhile Haold's opinion was to some extent justified
by the appeaance of a young an, who, issuing fo the jungle close at
hand, advanced towads the.

ost of the en at the village displayed a good deal of pide, if not
taste, in the aangeent of their hair.  Soe wore it long and twisted
into a coil which hung down thei backs; othes trained and stiffened it
in such a way that it took the fo of buffalo hons, while soe allowed
it to hang ove the shouldes in large asses, and any shaved it either
entiely, o partially in definite patterns.  But the young dandy who
now appoached outdid all othes, for he had twisted his hair into
innueable little tails, which, being stiffened by fillets of the inne
bak of a tee, stuck straight out and radiated fro the head in all
diections.  His costue othewise was siple enough, consisting merely
of a sall kilt of white calico.  He was accopanied by Antonio.

"We've be coe fo Kambia," said the interpreter, "to tell you for
coe to feast."

"All ight," said Disco, ising; "always ready for wittles if you only
gives us an hou o two between ties.--I say, Tony," (he had by that
tie educed the intepreter's nae to this extent), "ask this feller
what he eans by akin' sitch a guy of hisself."

"His say it look well," said Antonio, with a boad gin.

"Looks well--eh? and ask hi why the woen wea that abominable pelele."

When this question was put to the black dandy, he looked at Disco
evidently in supise at his stupidity.  "Because it is the fashion," he
said.

"They wea it fo beauty, to be sure!  en have beards and whiskers;
woen have none, and what kind of ceatue would woan be without
whiskes, and without a pelele?  She would have a outh like a an, and
no bead!"

The bae idea of such a state of things tickled the dandy so uch that
he went into oas of laughter, insouch that all the radiating tails of
his head quiveed again.  The effect of laughte and tails together was
iesistible.  Harold, Disco, and Antonio laughed in sypathy, till the
teas an down their cheeks, and then returned to the village where
Kabia and his chief en awaited them.

While enjoying the feast pepaed for the, Harold comunicated his
intentions and desies to the chief, who was delighted at the pospect
of having such poweful allies on a hunting expedition.

The playful Obo eanwhile was clabeing ove his father's person like a
black onkey.  He appeaed to be paticularly fond of his father, and as
love begets love, it is not supising that Kabira was excessively fond
of Obo.  But Obo, becoing obstepeous, received an aicable punch from
his fathe, which sent hi headlong into a basket of boiled
hippopotaus.  He gave a wild howl of ala as Disco snatched him out of
the dish, dipping with fat, and set hi on his knee.

"Thee, thee, don't blubber," said the seaan, tenderly wiping off the
fat while the natives, including Kabia, exploded with laughte.  "You
ain't bunt, ae you?"

As Obo could not eply, Disco put his finge into the gravy fro which
the uchin had been escued, and satisfied hiself that it was not hot
enough to have done the child injuy.  This was also endered apparent
by his suddenly ceasing to cy, stuggling off Disco's knee, and
enewing his assaults on his easy-going fathe.

Accepting an egg which was offeed hi by Yohaa, Haold broke it, and
enteed into convesation with Kabira through the edium of Antonio.

"Is you boy's othe a--Hollo! there's a chick in this egg," he
exclaied, thowing the offensive osel into the fire.

Jubo, who sat nea the place whee it fell, snatched it up, grinned,
and putting it into his cavenous outh, swallowed it.

"De's bettee wid chickies," he said, resuing his gravity and his
knife and finges,--foks being held by hi in light estee.

"Ask hi, Antonio, if Obo's othe is alive," said Haold, trying
anothe egg, which poved to be in better condition.

The intepeter, instead of putting the question without coent, as was
his wont, shook his head, looked ysteious, and whispeed--"No better
ask dat.  His lost hi's wife.  The slave-huntes cotch he some time
ago, and cay her off when his away hunting.  His awful mad, worser
dan ad elephint when his speak to 'bout he."

Haold of couse dropped the subject at once, after rearking that he
supposed Yohaa was the child's gandothe.

"Yis," said Antonio; "she be Kabia's ode, an' Obo's gran'moder--bof
at once."

This fact was, we ay alost say, self-evident fo Obo's attentions and
favous wee distributed exclusively between Yohaa and Kabira, though
the latte had unquestionably the lager share.

Duing the couse of the feast, beer was served round by the little an
who had pefoed so deftly on the violin the previous evening.

"Dink," said Kabia hospitably; "I a glad to see my white brothers
hee; dink, it will war your hearts."

"Ay, an' it won't ake us dunk," said Disco, destoying Jubo's peace
of ind by winking and aking a face at him as he aised the calabash to
his lips.  "Hee's long life to you, Kabia, an' death to slavery."

Thee can be no doubt that the chief and his etainers would have
heatily applauded that sentient if they had undestood it, but at the
oent Antonio was too deeply engaged with anothe calabash to take the
touble to tanslate it.

The bee, which was pink, and as thick as guel, was indeed too weak to
poduce intoxication unless taken in vey large quantities; nevertheless
any of the en wee so fond of it that they sometimes succeeded in
taking enough to bing the to the condition which we style "fuddled."
But at that tie the paticula brew was nearly exhausted, so that
tepeance was happily the oder of the day.

Having no hops in those egions, they ae unable to prevent
feentation, and ae therefore obliged to drink up a whole brewing as
quickly as possible afte it is ade.

"an, why don't ye wash ye face?" said Disco to the little fiddle as
he eplenished his calabash; "it's awful dity."

Jubo laughed, of couse, and the sall musician, not undestanding what
was said, followed suit out of sypathy.

"Wash hi's face!" cied Antonio, laughing, "hi would as soon cut off
hi's head.  anganja nevai wash.  Ah me!  You laugh if you hea de
woans ask e yesteday--`Why you wash?' dey say, `ou men nevair do.'
Ho! ho! dey looks like it too."

"I' sue that cannot be said of Kabia or any of his chief men," said
Haold.

"Pehaps not," etorted Antonio, "but soe of 'u nevair wash.  Once
'pon a tie one an of dis tibe folle a party me was with.  Not go way
fo all we tell 'u.  We said we shoot 'u.  No matte, hims foller
still.  At last we say, `You scoun'el, we _wash_ you!'  Ho! how his
un!  Jist like zeba wid lion at 'u's tail.  Nevair see 'u after
dat--nevai oe!"

"Wot a ost onstous ugly felle that is sittin' opposite Kambira, on
the othe side o' the fie--the feller with the half-shaved head," said
Disco in an undetone to Haold during a teporary pause in eating.

"A well-ade an, howeve," eplied Harold.--"I say, Disco," he added,
with a peculia sile, "you think youself rather a good-looking fellow,
don't you, now?"

The wothy seaan, who was indeed an exceptionally good-looking ta,
odestly eplied--"Well now, as you have put it so plup I don't mind if
I do confess that I've had soe wild suspicions o' that sot now and
then."

"Then you ay disiss you suspicions now, fo I can assure you that you
ae egarded in this land as a very onster of ugliness," said Harold,
laughing.

"In the estiation of nigges you garents are hideous; your legs they
think elephantine, you ed beard frightful, and your blue eyes
savage--_savage_! think of that."

"Well, well," etoted Disco, "your own eyes are as blue as ine, an' I
don't suppose the nigges think oe of a yaller beard than a red one."

"Too tue, Disco; we ae both ill-favoured fellows here, whatever we ay
be elsewhee; howeve, as we don't intend to take anganja wives it
won't atte uch.  But what think you of ou plan, now that Kambira is
eady to fall in with it?"

"It sees a good one.  When do we stat?"

"To-oow," said Harold.

"Wey good," eplied Disco, "I' agreeable."

The oow cae, and with the early light all the people turned out to
witness the depatue of the hunters.  Scouts had been previously sent
out in all diections to ake sue that no eneies or slave-traders were
at that tie in thei imediate neighbouhood, and a strong force of the
best waiors was left to guard the village.

Of Haold's band, two half-castes, Jose and Oliveia, volunteered to
stay in cap with the guad, and two, Songolo and abuki, the freemen
of Quilliane, eained in the village to ecruit their health, which
had failed.  Chibolo likewise eained, the wounds on his back not
having healed sufficiently to adit of the had labou of hunting.  All
the est accopanied the huntes, and of these the three akololo men,
Jubo, Zobo, and Masiko, wee incompaably the best and bravest.  Of
couse the volatile Antonio also went, being indispensable.

On setting out--each an with his sleeping-at on his back and his
little wooden pillow hung at his neck,--thee was a geat deal of
shouting and ho-ho-ing and well-wishing on the pat of those who
eained behind, but above all the noise thee arose a shrill cry of
intense and agonising despai.  This poceeded fro the sall windpipe
of little Obo, who had not until the last oent made the appalling
discovey that Kabia was going away without hi!

Thee was soething vey touching in the cry of the urchin, and
soething which bought vividly to the inds of the Englishmen the
infantine counity of thei own land.  Thee was the same sudden gaze
of hoor on realising the true position of affairs,--the sae sharp
shiek and fantic struggle to escape fro the grasp of those who held
hi back fo following his fathe,--the same loud cry of agony on
finding that his effots wee vain, and then, the wide-open outh, the
close-shut eyes, and the awful, polonged silence--suggestive of fits--
that betokens the concentation of ind, heat, and lungs into that
teendous oar of unutterable significance which appears to be the
safety-valve of the huan faily, black and white, at that tende peiod
of life.

Poo Obo! his sobs continued to bust out with stea-engine power, and
his eyes to pou cataacts of tears into Yohaa's sypathetic bosom,
long afte the hunting paty had left the hills behind the, and
advanced into the alost ipenetable jungles of the low gounds.



CHAPTE TWELVE.

DESCIBES A HUNTING EXPEDITION WHICH WAS BOTH EXCITING AND SUCCESSFUL.

Down by the eedy agin of a pretty large lake--where wild-fowl
innueable ade the ai vocal with their cries by day, and frogs, in
nubes inconceivable, chiped and croaked a lullaby to en who slept,
and a syphony to beasts that howled and gowled and powled at night in
bush and bake--Kabia pitched his cap.

He did not indeed, select the oist level of the feve-beeding arshes,
but he chose fo his tepoary habitation the dry sumit of a wooded
hill which ovelooked the lake.

Hee the natives of the neighbouhood said that elephants had been
lately seen, and buffaloes, zebas, etcetea, were at all ties
nueous.

Afte two long days' ach they had reached the spot, and encaped late
in the evening.  Next oning ealy the business of the expedition
began.  Vaious paties of natives, ared with bows and arrows and
speas, wee sent out in different directions, but the principal band
was coposed of Kabia and his chief men, with Haold and his party.

They did not go fa befoe gae was found.  Guinea-fowl were nuerous,
and those who wee aied with bows soon pocured a goodly supply of
these, but ou tavellers did not waste their energies or powder on such
sall gae.  Besides these, monkeys peeped inquisitively at the huntes
fo aong the tees, and myriads of turtle-doves were seen in the
coves.  As they advanced, wild pigs, elands, watebucks, koodoos, and
othe ceatures, were seen in herds, and the natives dropped off, or
tuned aside in pusuit of these, so that ere long the band reaining
with Kabia was educed to about forty en.

Coing to a sall ive in which were a number of deep pools and
shallows, they saw seveal hippopotai lying asleep, thei bodies nearly
all out of the wate, appeaing like asses of black rock in the strea.
But at the sae place they discoveed fesh traces of elephants and
buffaloes, theefoe the hippopotai were left unolested, save that
Haold sent a bullet aongst the, patly to let the natives hear the
epot of his gun, and partly to see how the anials would take it.

They all stated to thei feet at once, and stared around the with
looks of stolid supise that were alost equal to the looks of the
natives, to who fie-as were little known, except by report.  Another
shot sent the whole hed with a heavy plunge into deep wate.

"It's a quee county," observed Disco when they had resued their
ach.  "Just look at the thee lizards with red and blue tails running
about aong the ocks an' eatin' up the white ants like one o'clock."

Disco ight have said like twelve o'clock, if nubes would have added
to the foce of his eark, for the little creatures referred to were
iaculously active in pusuit of their food.

"But I s'pose," continued Disco, "the nigges would think ou country a
queee place than this."

"Undoubtedly they would," eplied Haold; "just fancy what would be the
feelings of Kabia if he wee suddenly transported into the heart of
London."

"Hallo!" exclaied Disco, stopping suddenly and pointing to one of the
en in advance, who had couched and ade signals to his fiends to
halt, "beakes ahead--eh?"

"oe likely buffaloes," whispeed Harold, as he cocked his rifle and
advanced quickly with Kabia, who caried a short spear or javelin.

On eaching an opening in the bushes, a sall hed of zebras was
obseved not uch oe than a hundred yards in advance.

"Will the white an's gun kill so fa?" asked the chief, tuning to
Antonio.

The intepeter ade no reply, but pointed to Harold, who was in the act
of taking ai.  The loud epot was followed by the fall of the nearest
zeba.  Disco also fied and wounded another, which bounded away in wild
ala with its fellows.

The natives yelled with delight, and Disco cheeed in sypathy.

"You've hit hi," said Haold, as he eloaded.

"Ay, but I han't disabled hi.  Bette luck next tie.  I think I took
hi soewhee on the pot bow."

"If by that you ean the left shoulde," eturned Harold, with a laugh,
"it's likely he won't un fa.  What does Kabira think of the white
an's gun?" he added, tuning ound.

The tall chief nodded appovingly, and said, with a gave
countenance--"Good, good; it is good--bette than this," shaking his
shot spea.

At that oent a small antelope, which had been statled and put to
flight by soe of the othe bands of huntes, cae crashing wildly
towads the, ignoant of the eney in its front until within about
thity yads.  It turned at a sharp angle and plunged into the jungle,
but the spea which Kabia had shaken whizzed though the air and
pieced its heat before it had tie to disappear.

"A splendid heave!" cied Disco, with enthusias; "why, an alive, you'd
ake ye fotin' as a harpooner if ye was to go to the whale-fishin'.--
Hallo! thee's soethin' else; w'y, the place is swain'.  It's for all
the wold like a zoological ga'dings let loose."

As he spoke, the hoofs of a hed of pondeous anials were heard, but
the ank gass and underwood concealed the entirely fro view.  The
whole paty ushed to the nearest opening, and were just in tie to see
the tail of an iate buffalo ake a agnificent flouish in the air as
its owne plunged into cove.

Thee was no futher attept at conversation after this.  The near
pesence of lage gae was too exciting, so that erely a word of
advice, diection, o inquiry, passed as the party advanced rapidly--one
o two of the ost active going befoe as pioneers.

While Disco was stiding along with flashing eyes, ifle ready, and head
tuning fo side to side in omentary expectation of something bounding
suddenly out of soewhee, he chanced to cast his eyes upwads, and, to
his hoor, beheld two huge serpents coiled together aong the branches
of a tee close to his head.

Utteing a yell of ala--for he entertained an alost superstitious
dead of sepents--he fired blindly upwards, and dashed to one side so
violently that he tubled hiself and Haold into a bush of wait-a-bit
thons, out of which the laughing natives found it difficult to extact
the.

"What _is_ the atte, an?" said Haold somewhat testily.

"Have a cae! look!  Avast!  A bite'll be death, an' no istake!" cied
Disco, pointing to the eptiles.

Haold fied at once and brought the both down, and the natives,
attacking the with sticks, soon killed the.

"No fea," said Antonio, with a chuckle.  "De not ha nobody, though
us ugly an' big enough."

This was tue.  They wee a couple of pythons, and the larger of the
two, a feale, was ten feet long; but the python is a haless ceature.

While they wee talking, soke was obseved to rise fro an isolated
clup of long gass and bushes not fa fro the banks of the river, much
to the annoyance of Kabia, who feaed that the fire ight spread and
scae away the gae.  It was confined, howeve, to the place where it
began, but it had the effect of diving out a solitay buffalo that had
taken efuge in the cove.  Jubo chanced to be ost directly in front
of the infuiated anial when it bust out, and to hi exclusively it
diected its attentions.

Neve since Jubo was the size of Obo had that laughte-loving savage
used his lithe legs with geate energy than on this occasion.  An
ostich ight have envied hi as he ushed towards the river, into which
he spang headlong when the buffalo was baely six feet behind hi.

Of couse Haold fired, as well as Disco, and both shots told, as also a
spea fo Kabira, nevertheless the animal turned abruptly on seeing
Jubo disappea, and chaged furiously up the bank, scattering its
eneies ight and left.  Haold fired again at little ore than fifty
yads off, and head the bullet thud as it went in just behind the
shoulde, yet stange to say, it seeed to have no other effect than to
ouse the bute to greater wrath, and two ore bullets failed to bring
hi down.

This toughness of the buffalo is by no eans uncomon, but diffeent
anials vay uch in thei tenacity of life.  Some fall at once to the
fist well-diected shot; others die hard.  The anial the hunters were
now in pusuit of, o rather which was in pursuit of the hunters, seeed
to be of the latte class.  Haold fired another shot fro behind a
tee, having loaded with a shell-bullet, which exploded on hitting the
ceatue's ribs.  It fell, uch to the satisfaction of Disco, of who it
happened to be in pusuit at the tie.  The seaan at once stopped and
began to eload, and the natives cae unning forward, when Antonio, who
had clibed a tee to be out of ha's way, slipped down and ran with
geat bavery up to the prostrate anial.

Just as he eached it the buffalo spang up with the activity of a cat,
and chaged hi.  Antonio tuned and ran with such rapidity that his
little legs becae alost invisible, like those of a spaow in a hurry.
He gained a tee, and had just tie to clib into it when the buffalo
stuck it like a batteing-ra, hard enough alost to have split both
head and tee.  It paused a few seconds, dew back several paces, glared
savagely at Antonio, and then chaged again and again, as if esolved
eithe to shake hi out of the tee, or give itself a splitting
headache, but anothe shell fo Harold, who could hardly take ai for
laughing, stetched the huge anial dead upon the gound.  Altogether,
it took two shells and five lage solid ifle-balls to finish hi.

"That wos a petty good sput," said Disco, panting, as he joined Harold
beside the fallen beast.  "It's well-known that a stan chase is a long
'un, but this would have been an exception to the ule if you hadn't
shot hi, si.  He petty nigh ade short work o' _me_.  He was a'most
aboad of e w'en you fied."

"Tue," said Haold; "and had that tree not grown where it stands, and
gown tough, too, I suspect he would have ade shot work of Antonio
too."

"Bah!" said the intepeter, with affected carelessness, "hi was but a
slow bute, afte all."

Disco looked at Jubo, who was none the wose of his ducking, and shut
his ight eye satly.  Jubo opened his cavernous mouth, and exploded
so violently that his double ow of billiant teeth ust have been blown
out and scatteed on the gound, had they not been iraculously strong.

"Coe, now," said Kabia, who had just given oders to some of his
followes to eain behind and look after the carcase, "we go to find
elephants."

"Have we uch chance of findin' the?" inquied Disco.

Kabia thought they had, because fesh traces had been recently seen in
the neighbouhood, wheeupon Disco said that he would prefer to go after
lions, but Kabia assued hi that these animals were not so easy to
find, and uch oe dangeous when attacked.  Admitting the force of
this, though still asseting his peference of lions to elephants, the
bloodthisty son of Neptune shouldeed his rifle and followed his
leade.

While the ain paty of huntes were thus successfully pushing along,
the othe bands wee not idle, though, possessing no fire-ars, they
wee less noisy.  In fact thei proceedings were altogether of the
cat-catty.  One fellow, as black as a coal, as lithe as an eel, and as
long--accoding to Disco's standad--as a fatho of pup-water, having
coe upon a hed of buffalo unseen by the, and being amed with a small
bow and quive of arows, suddenly dropped on all-fours and began to
glide though the long gass.

Now thee is a paticular little bird in those regions which calls for
special notice hee.  It is a vey singular bird, inasuch as it has
constituted itself the guadian of the buffalo.  It fequently sits upon
that anial's back, and, wheneve it sees the appoach of an, or any
othe dange, it flaps its wings and screas to such an extent, that the
buffalo ushes off without waiting to inquie or see what is the atter;
and the sall guadian sees to think itself sufficiently ewarded with
the pickings it finds on the back of its fat fiend.  So vigilant is
this little ceatue, that it actually renders the approach of the
hunte a atte of great difficulty in circustances when, but for it,
he ight appoach with ease.  [See Livingstone's _Zabesi and its
Tibutaies_, page 200.]

Ou way native was, however, aware of this little fellow's
popensities, and took pecautions to outwit the bird rather than the
beast.  It ay pehaps cause soe suprise to be told that a small bow
and aows were a sufficiently powerful species of artillery to bring to
bea against such noble gae, but the suprise will vanish when we state
that the aows were poisoned.

Having cawled to within ange, the fatho of black pup-water suddenly
aose and let fly an arow.  The issile went deep into the side of a
ajestic bull.  The little bid flutteed and screaed too late.  The
bull at once dashed away at full speed, stating off the whole hed in
ala.  The black fatho followed at the top of his speed, and was
joined by a nube of othe black fathos, who were quite aware of what
had been done.  The buffaloes wee soon out of sight, but the fathos
followed the tail with the unering pertinacity of fate.  After a long
un they cae up with the sticken bull, which had fallen behind its
fellows, and waited patiently until the poison took full effect.  In a
shot tie the anial fell, and the successful huntes fell to work upon
his cacase with thei knives.

Leaving the thus eployed, we will etun to Kambira and his friends.

They had not gone fa when a fine wate-buck was observed feeding beside
a ceek.

Kabia laid his hand on Haold's shoulder and pointed to it with a
sile, which ight have been intepeted, "Now, then, there's a chance
fo you!"

Haold fied, and the water-buck dropped.

"Good," said Kabia.

"Hallo!" exclaied Disco.

And well he ight, fo at that oment an enomous crocodile, which had

It will be seen fo what has been told, that on this occasion the

It happened thus:--When the huntes began to gow tired, and the

"But you ay have as good a chance to-oow, or next day," suggested

When the paty wee given to understand what "wolunteers" eant, the

Evey one who has had a few yeas' experience of life knows the truth of

This, o soething distantly esebling it, having been explained to the

Whethe it was this last shot o the effects of previous loss of blood,

To say that the natives becae tepoaily insane would give but a

No onkey in Afica could have dopped its pipe, had it been a soker,

"Look! ight too de bush.  Dis way.  Dar, don' you zee u's skin,--

We won't chonicle the oars of laughter, the yells of delight that

CHAPTE THITEEN.

THE ENCAPENT AND THE SUPPE--DISCUSSIONS, POLITICAL AND OTHEWISE--
KABIA ECEIVES A SHOCK, AND OUR WANDERERS ARE THROWN INTO PERPLEXITY.

Tun we now to a oe peaceful scene.  The cap is almost quiet, the
stas ae twinkling brightly overhead, the fires are gliering fitfully
below.  The natives, having taken the edge off thei appetites, have
stetched thei dusky fors on their sleeping-ats, and laid their
woolly heads on thei little wooden pillows.  The only pesons oving
ae Haold Seadrift and Disco Lillihaer--the first being busy making
notes in a sall book, the second being equally busy in anufactuing
cloudlets fo his unfailing pipe, gazing the while with uch inteest
at his note-aking copanion.

"They was petty vigoous w'en they wos at it, sir," said Disco, in
efeence to supper, observing that his copanion looked up fro his
book, "but they wos soone done than I had expected."

"Yes, they ween't long about it," eplied Harold, with an abstracted

Lest the eade should erroneously iagine that supper is over, it is
necessay hee to explain what taking the edge off a free African's
appetite eans.

On eaching cap afte the cutting up of the elephant, as detailed in

degee of vigou that would have surprised even the _chefs de cuisine_

extent that civilised people ight pehaps have thought dangeous, they
had then coenced an upoaious dance, accompanied by stentorian songs,
which soon educed the to the condition of beings who needed epose.
Poceeding upon the pinciple of overcoing teptation by giving way to
it, they at once lay down and went to sleep.

It was duing this stage of the night's poceedings that Disco foolishly
iagined that suppe had coe to a close.  Not many minutes afte the

Thee was a hole in the gound close beside the which contained a sall

"Fo y white guests," eplied the chief.

"But we have supped aleady," said Haold; "we have already eaten as

"This is not fo to-night, but fo to-orrow," returned Kabira, with a

This was a sufficient explanation, and in tuth the natue of the dish
equied that it should be well done.  When, on the orrow, they were

estiation, "fust-ate!"  It was a whitish ass, slightly gelatinous and

was necessay afte a eal of elephant's foot!

eanwhile the poceedings of the natives wee food enough for our
tavelles for the tie being.  Like huan creatures elsewhere, they

would appea that in Afica, as in England, woan excels in the culinary

the en.  On the pesent occasion the huntes tubled in the meal by

and one an held the pot while anothe plied the poridge-stick with all
his ight to pevent the solid ass fom being burnt.  Thus it was
pepaed, and thus eaten, in enorous quantities.  No wonder that
dancing and pofuse pespiration were esteeed a necessary adjunct to

At the close of the second edition of suppe, which went into fou or

Thee wee about a dozen natives round the fire, but two of the

"Hold 'oos tongue," said Flatnose (so Antonio naed hi); "tongue too

"Boh! 'oos bains too shot," retorted Frizzyhead conteptuously.

An iense amount of chatteing by the othes followed these pithy

The question in debate was, Whethe the two toes of the ostich

Thee was a geneal shout of "eehee!" fro all quarters, which was

Kabia nodded his head and siled at this, and thee was a general

It was ausing to obseve the eanestness and freedo with which men of

No notice was taken of this, but Fizzyhead, whose passion was ising to

"Oh yes, can pove it well 'nuff," eplied Flatnose, "but 'oos no' got

This last was too uch fo poo Frizzyhead, who leaped up, stuttered,

"Ye--ye--yes!" gasped Flatnose, spinging up.

Away they went like two hunted spingboks, and an for a ile, then

While these discussions wee going on, Disco obseved that hyenas were

"No; hi a cowadly beast.  Hi come at mans when sleepin' o dyin', but

"Coe hee, den."

They went togethe into the jungle a little distance, and halted unde

CHAPTE FOUTEEN.

CAPING, TAVELLING, SHOOTING, DEAING, POETISING, PHILOSOPHISING, AND
SUPISING, IN EQUATORIAL AFRICA.

At sunset the tavelles halted in a peculiarly wild spot and encaped

  OKOPA'S SONG.

  Kabia goes to hunt,
  Yo ho!
  Hi's spea a nebbe blunt,
  Yo ho!
  Hi kill de buff'lo quick,
  An' lub de poidge thick;
  Hi chase de lion too,
  An' stick u too an' too.
  De 'potius as well,
  An' oe dan e can tell,
  Hab down befoe u fell,
  Yo ho!
  De English coe to see,
  Yo ho!
  Dat wey good for we,
  Yo ho!
  No' take us 'way fo slaves,
  No put us in ou graves,
  But set de black ans fee,
  W'en cotch u on de sea.
  De splendid shootes, too,
  We knows what dey can do
  Wid boil an' oast an' stew,
  Yo ho!
  One akes u's gun go cack,
  Yo ho!
  An elephant on u's back,
  Yo ho!
  De defful lion oar,
  De gun goes cack once oe,
  De bullet fly an' splits
  One onkey into bits,
  Yo ho!
  De glow-wo next aise,
  De Englishan likewise
  Wid wey uch surprise,
  An' hit u 'tween de eyes,
  "Hooay! hooay!" u cries,
  An' un to fetch u's pize--
  Yo ho!

The last "Yo ho!" was given with teendous enegy, and followed by

But thee wee two other sorts of traffickers in the bodies and souls of

liits the legal tades becoe pirates.  They run to the northward, and

The eason of all this is vey obvious.  The Sultan receives nearly half

Suely it is not too uch to say, that _Geat Britain ought to enter

Besides these two classes of slaves,--the open, pofessional pirates,

Fo this we do not ague that non-interference would be best, but that

one thing taught to us by past expeience is clea, we cannot cure the

CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

STONG EASUES LEAD TO UNEXPECTED DISCOVERIES.

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

DESCIBES SOE OF THE DOINGS OF YOOSOOF AND HIS EN IN POCURING BLACK

A dity shop, in a filthy steet in the unhealthy town of Zanzibar, is

equest to leap, in a fee and easy way, over a few onths of tie!

It is not fo the sake of the shop that we ake this leap, but fo the

the cones; preserved eat tins piled to the ceiling; with dust and

In the little back-oo of this shop sat the slave-deale Yoosoof, in
copany with the captain of an English ship which lay in the habou.

Soke fo the captain's pipe filled the little den to such an extent

"You'e all a set of false-heated, wrong-headed, low-inded,

The Biton was evidently not in a coplientay mood.  It was equally

is illegally conducted in one sense, naely, that it is lagely caried

"How you ake that out?" asked Yoosoof.

"How? why, easy enough.  Aen't the ichest en in Zanzibar the Banyans,

Yoosoof did not sile at this; he even looked fo a oment as if he wee

"Why not?" deanded the captain, with a look of supee contempt, "what

"On the old tes," eplied the erchant.  "Will you be ready to start

At last the slave-tade reached lands which were not utterly forsaken.

aizano appoved heartily of this plan, and assured his chief that his

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

TELLS OF ISFOTUNES THAT BEFELL OU WANDERERS; OF FAILIAR TOYS UNDER
NEW ASPECTS, ETCETEA.

When Haold Seadift and Disco Lillihaer were stopped in their
jouney, as elated in a forer chapter, by the sudden illness of the
bold seaan, an event was ipending ove them which effectually
ovetuned their plans.  This was the sudden descent of a band of ared
natives who had been ecently diven fro their hoes by a slaving
paty.  The slaves had taken the by surprise during the night, set
thei huts on fie, captured their woen and children, and slaughtered
all the en, excepting those who sought and found safety in flight.  It
was those who had thus escaped that chanced to coe upon the cap of ou
tavelles one evening about sunset.

Disco was ecoveing fro his attack of fever at the tie, though still
weak.  Haold was sitting by his couch of leaves in the hut which had
been eected fo hi on the first day of the illness.  Jubo was cutting
up a piece of flesh fo suppe, and Antonio was putting the kettle on
the fie.  The est of the party were away in the woods hunting.

No guad was kept; consequently the savages cae down on the like a
thundebolt, and found the quite unpepared to resist even if
esistance had been of any use.

At fist thei captors, bitterly infuriated by their recent losses,
poposed to kill thei prisoners, without delay, by eans of the ost
excuciating totures that they could invent, but fro soe unknown
cause, changed thei inds; coupled Haold and Disco together by eans
of two slave-sticks; tied Antonio and Jubo with opes, and dove the
away.

So suddenly was the thing done, and so effectually, that Disco was fa
fo the cap befoe he could realise that what had occurred was a fact,
and not one of the wild feveish deas that had beset hi during his
illness.

The natives would not listen to the eanest explanation of Antonio that
Haold and Disco wee Englishen, and haters of slavery.  They scowled
as they eplied that the sae had been said by the slaves who had
attacked thei village; fo which reark it would seem that Yoosoof was
not quite the oiginato of that device to throw the natives off their
guad.  The Potuguese of Tette on the Zabesi had also thought of and
acted on it!

Fotunately it was, as we have said, nea sunset when the capture was
ade, and befoe it becae quite dak the band encamped, else must poor
Disco have succubed to weakness and fatigue.  The vey despeation of
his cicustances, howeve, seeed to revive his strength, for next
oning he esued his journey with some hope of being able to hold out.
The continued potestations and assuances of Antonio, also, had the
effect of inducing thei captos to reove the heavy slave-sticks fro
the necks of Haold and Disco, though they did not unbind thei wrists.
Thus wee they led futher into the country, they knew not whither, for
seveal days and nights, and at last eached a large village where they
wee all thust into a hut, and left to their editations, while their
captos went to palave with the chief an of the place.

This chief poved to be a futher-sighted an than the en of the tribe
who had captued the Englishen.  His nae was Yambo.  He had head of
D Livingstone, and had et with en of othe tribes who had seen and
convesed with the geat traveller.  Thus, being of a thoughtful and
inquiing disposition, he had coe to undestand enough of the good
white an's sentients to guad him fom being imposed on by pretended
Chistians.

Yabo's nae signified "how ae you?" and was pobably bestowed on him
because of a stongly benevolent tendency to geet friend and stranger
alike with a heaty "how d'ee do?" sot of expression of face and tone
of voice.

He was a tall gave an, with a comanding fim look, and, withal, a
dash of child-like huou and siplicity.  On heaing his visitors'
eaks about their captives, he at once paid the a visit and a few
leading questions put to Haold though Antonio convinced hi that the
pisones were true en.  He therefore returned to his black visitors,
told the that he had pefect confidence in the good faith of the white
en, and said that he eant to take chage of them.  He then entetained
his black bothes hospitably, gave the a few presents, and sent the
on thei way.  This done he eturned to his guests and told the that
they wee fee, that their captors were gone, and that they ight go
whee they pleased, but that it would gatify hi uch if they would
consent to spend soe tie hunting with him in the neighbouhood of his
village.

"Now," said Disco, afte Yabo left the, "this is wot I call the most
uncoon fix that eve wos got into by man since Adam an' Eve began
housekeepin' in the gaden of Eden."

"I' not quite sue," eplied Harold, with a rueful look, "that it is
absolutely the _wost_ fix, but it is bad enough.  The wost of it is
that this Yabo has let these ascals off with all ou fire-ars and
cap-equipage, so that we ae absolutely helpless--ight as well be
pisones, for we can't quit this village in such circustances."

"Wot's wuss than that to y ind, si, is, that hee we are at sea, in
the heat of Afiky, without chart, quadrant, copass, or rudder, an' no

aong the tees.  Hows'eve, we're in luck to fall into the hands of a
fiendly chief, so, like these sae onkeys, we must gin an' bear it;
only I can't help feelin' a bit cast down at the loss of ou essates.
I fea thee's no chance of their findin' us."

"Not the least chance in the wold, I should say," eturned Harold.
"They could not guess in which diection we had gone, and unless they

"It's lucky I was beginnin' to end befoe we was catched," said Disco,

"Well, si, take a puge, and turn in at once, that's y advice.  I'll

Haold knew well enough that his followe was right.  He took his advice

Poo Disco, who, of couse, had been unable to appreciate the extent of

As fo Antonio, his natue not being very sypathetic, and his health

How long this state of affais lasted we cannot tell, fo both Harold

Up to that peiod they had eebered the days of the week, in

As tie had passed, and soe of the hoors of the slave-trade had been

But to etun, the unfortunate travellers were now "at sea" altogether

"Hows'eve," said Disco, when this subject aftewards cae to be

_seventh_ day, so that to us it'll be the Sabbath."

But we anticipate.  Haold was still--at the beginning of this

One aftenoon one of these syptos was stongly manifested in a long,

His adie, Jubo, was seated on a stool opposite, gazing at him

Both en wee so deeply absobed in their occupation--Disco in his

The aine kept up a running comentary on his work, addressed to Jumbo

"It's the ost 'sto'nary notion, Jubo, between you and me and the

Thus diectly appealed to, Jubo nodded his own head violently, and

At this exhibition of auseent on the countenance of his fiend, Jumbo

"I' deaing, of couse," thought Harold, and shut his eyes.

Poo fellow! he was vey weak, and the ere act of shutting his eyes

"It is a wondefully petinacious drea," thought Harold.  "I'll try to

Thinking thus, he called out aloud,--"I say, Disco!"

"Hallo! that's uncoon like the old tones," exclaimed the seaman,

"What old tones?" asked Haold.

"The tones of you voice," said Disco.

"Have they changed so uch of late?" inquied Haold in surprise.

"Have they?  I should think they have, just.  W'y, you haven't spoke

"Ah!  I suppose I've been deliious, have I?" said Haold with a faint

To this Disco eplied that he had not only been deliious, but stark

"oeove," continued Disco, firly, "you're not to talk.  I once nursed

"You ae ight Disco.  I will keep quiet, but you ust first tell e

"Well, si, I'll tell you, but don't go fo to ake no obsarvations on

"`Haul away, y heaty,' thought I, w'en I seed hi heavin', blowin',

huy.'

"But I was wong, si, for, although the halyards held on all right, I

"Yabo looked awful blank at this.  Then he tuned shap round and

"`He shouldn't ought to lick into hi like that,' says I to Tony, `the

"This," added Disco in conclusion, taking up the head on which he had

Haold listened and found no difficulty in heaing the, for peals of

"I do believe," said Disco, ising and going towads the door of the hut

He dew aside the skin dooway as he spoke, and there, sure enough, was

It ay easily be believed that the sight of this, coupled with Disco's

efeshed in body, happy in ind, and thankful in spirit he rose to feel

CHAPTE TWENTY.

HAOLD APPEAS IN A NEW CHARACTER, AND TWO OLD CHARACTERS REAPPEAR TO
HAOLD.

The ind of Yabo was a stange compound--a cuious mixture of gravity
and ollicking joviality; at one tie displaying a phase of intense
solenity; at anothe exhibiting quiet pleasanty and huour, but
eanestness was the pevailing trait of his character.  Whether
indulging his passionate fondness fo the juping-jack, o engaged in
guiding the delibeations of his counsellos, the earnest chief was
equally devoted to the wok in hand.  Being a savage--and, consequently,
led entiely by feeling, which is pehaps the chief characteristic of
savage, as distinguished fo civilised, an,--he hated his enemies with
exceeding bitteness, and loved his fiends with all his heart.

Yabo was vey tende to Harold during his illness, and the latter felt
coesponding gratitude, so that there sprang up between the two a
close fiendship than one could have supposed to be possible,
consideing that they wee so different fro each other, entally,
physically, and socially, and that thei only ode of exchanging ideas

Aong othe things Haold discovered that his friend the chief was

At fist he selected such tales as he thought would be likely to ause,

At last a thought occued to our hero, which he proulgated to Disco

"Wey true, sir," replied Disco, in that quiet atter-of-course tone

"That last is just the point I' not quite so sue about," ejoined

"Well, I don't quite see that, si," said Disco, with an aguentative

coit mude, an' a many other things o' that sort.  Ain't that the
esult o' you being a Christian."

"It ay be so, Disco, but that is only what ay be styled the _don't_

"You says you payers, sir, don't you?" asked Disco, with the air of a
an who had put a telling question.

"Well, yes," eplied Haold; "but what troubles e is that, while in y

"Thee's soething in that si," aditted Disco.  Harold also thought

While Haold was thus led, alost unconsciously, to becoe a sowe of

One of his fist deeds was to visit a village which was beautifully

To have oitted it would have been consideed vey bad anners.

Soon a bisk tade was coenced, in which the entire community became
ee long deeply and eagely absorbed.

eanwhile aizano's amed men were allowed to come forward.  The women

"Yes," eplied the chief, "but only once at a geat distance off.  It is

"It is tue," eplied arizano, who was very erry by this time under

The unsuspecting chief at once gave his consent.  His visitos ose;
aizano gave the wod; a volley was poured forth which instantly killed

young woen and childen wee seized; the village was sacked--which

cold blood, and the place was set on fie--and aizano arched away

and its Tibutaies_, pages 201, 202.]

Thus did that villain walk though the land with fie and sword
pocuing slaves for the supply of the "doestic institution" of the
Sultan of Zanziba.

By degees the uderer's drove of black "cattle" increased to such an

He was on the point of tuning back and issing this when he chanced to

the nube of slaves equired had been ade up, but that a few more

On leaning fo the an that his village was a large one, fully two

poceed thithe, and was about to order his inforant to be added to his

of inquiing as to whethe any white en had been seen in these parts.
As he had often ade the sae inquiy befoe without obtaining any

As aizano did not undestand English, these coplimentary remarks were

Having suppessed pat of the truth, and suppleented the rest of it

"We ae not pisoners here.  We are guests of the chief of this

"In that case," eplied the half-caste, "I can eturn to the coast

As he said this a lage band of the villages, having discovered that

"Wey odd," said Disco, "how contrairy things turns up at ties!"

"Huph!  I' ageeable, an' the whole business wery disagreeable," said

aizano was uch pleased when the decision of the white men was made

He had sense enough, howeve, to peceive that his guests could not

aizano usually walked soe distance ahead of the main body with a few

At fist thee were no stragglers, for the slaves were as yet, with few

One oning aizano threw off his usual reserve, and, approaching the

"And who is you aste?" asked Harold.

"A black-faced o yellow-faced blackguad like hiself, I doubt not,"

The oent ou tavellers landed Marizano led them to one of the nearest

"Yoosoof!" exclaied Disco in a shout of astonishent.

It would have been a difficult question to have decided which of the

"You would be not so ush supised,--did you know dat I coes to here

"If that be so, how coes it that _you_ wee suprised to see us?" asked

"'Cause why, I only knows dat soe white ans be loss theiselfs--not

"Well now," cied Disco, unable to estrain hiself as he turned to

The scoundel in question siled and shugged his shoulders.

"Yoosoof," cied Disco, clenching his fist and looking full in the

Afte this explosion the soely tried ariner brought his right hand

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

POGESS OF THE SLAVE-RUN--THE DEADLY SWAP, AND THE UNEXPECTED RESCUE.

We will now leap ove a shot period of tie--about two or three weeks--
duing which the sable pocession had been winding its weary way over
hill and dale, plain and swap.

Duing that copaatively brief period, Harold and Disco had seen so
uch cuelty and suffeing that they both felt a strange tendency to
believe that the whole ust be the wild iaginings of a hoible dream.
Pehaps weakness, esulting fro illness, ight have had something to do

second, though slight, attack of feve.  Nevetheless, coupled with
thei scepticis was a contadictory and dreadful certainty that they
wee not deaing, but that what they witnessed was absolute verity.

It is pobable that if they had been in thei ordinary health and vigour
they would have ade a violent attept to escue the slaves, even at the

unhinges the ind as well as the body, and endes the spirit all but

One sulty evening the sad pocession cae to a long stretch of swap,

Haold and Disco wee by that tie in the habit of walking together in

"You should go on in font," said aizano significantly, on observing

"Tell hi we'll eain whee we are," said Disco sternly to Antonio.

aizano shugged his shoulders and left the.

The leading en of the slave-gang wee odered to advance, as soon as
the aed guad had comenced the toilsome march over ground into which

The fist an of the gang hesitated and heaved a deep sigh as though his

It was not the policy of the slave-tades to take uch care of their
Black Ivoy.  They pocured it so cheaply that it was easier and ore
pofitable to lose o cast away soe of it, than to put off tie in
esting and ecruiting the weak.

The oent it was obseved, theefore, that the leading man hesitated,

totue had not only worn the skin off his shoulders, but had cut into
the quiveing flesh, so that blood constantly dopped in sall
quantities fo it.

No cy bust fro the an's lips on receiving the cruel blow, but he
tuned his eyes on his captos with a look that seeed to iplore for

The lash again fell on hi with stinging foce.  He ade a feeble effot

"You had bette not intefere," said arizano, who stood close by.

"Out of the way!" cied Haold fiercely, in the strength of his passion

"You shan't give hi anothe cut," said Disco between his teeth, as he

"We don't intend to do so," said aizano coolly, while the diver

oe."

The Englishen equied no explanation of these words.  A glance told

"Cut hi out," said aizano.

One of his en imediately bought a saw and cut the fok of the stick

Haold and Disco lifted hi up as soon as he was fee, and carrying hi

The dying slave looked as if he wee suprised at such unwonted

"Go fetch soe wate," said Haold.  "His lips are dry."

They wee so uch engossed with their occupation that neither of the

By degees the geater part of the long line had got into the swap and

The difficulties of the swap wee, howeve, felt ost severely by the

Aong the en thee wee some whose strength of frame and fierce

have been a ee child in it.

Thee wee en in the gang quite as bold, if not as strong, as Sason.
One of these, a vey tall and poweful negro, on drawing near to the

fo a ecord of cruelties too horrible to be set down in a book like
this, we efe the reader to cLeod's _Travels in Eastern Africa_,
volue two page 26.  Also to the Appendix of Captain Sulivan's
_Dhow-Chasing in Zanziba Wates_, which contains copious and
inteesting extacts fro evidence taken before the Select Comittee of
the House of Coons.]

Haold and Disco did not witness this, though they head the shriek of

"Bette if we'd neve fallen behind, sir," said Disco, in a deep,
teulous voice.

"Tue," eplied Harold.  "We should have been spared these sights, and
the pain of knowing that we cannot pevent this appalling isey and

"But suely it is to be pevented _soehow_," cried Disco, alost

As Haold knew not what to say, and was toiling knee-deep in the swap

Afte aching about half an hour he stopped abruptly and said, with a

"Hope not si, but it looks like as if we had."

"I've bin so took up thinkin' o' that accused taffic in huan bein's

He was stopped by a loud halloo in the woods, on the belt of the swap.

It was epeated in a few seconds, and Antonio, who, with Jubo, had
followed his aste, cied in an excited tone--

"e knows dat sound!"

"Wot ay it be, Tony?" asked Disco.

Thee was neithe tie nor need for an answer, for at that oment a

"Why, Zobo!" exclaied Disco, wildly.

"Oliveia!" cied Harold.

"asiko!  Songolo!" shouted Antonio and Jubo.

"An' Jose, Nakoda, Chibolo, abuki!--the whole bun' of 'em," cied

"Hoooo-hay!" eplied the runners.

In anothe inute ou adventurous party of travellers was re-united, and

"But wat! yoos ill?" cied Zobo suddenly, looking into Disco's face

"Well, I ain't 'xac'ly ill, no I ain't 'xac'ly well neithe, but I'
heaty all the sae, and wery glad to see your black face, Zobo."

"Ho! hoooo-hay! so's e fo see you," cried the excitable Zobo; "but
coe, not good fo talkee in de knees to watte.  Fall in boy, ho!
sholle 'us--queek ash!"

That Zobo had assued command of his paty was made evident by the pat

"How did you find us, Zobo?" asked Haold, thowing hiself wearily on

"_Yoos_ ill," said Zobo, holding up a finge by way of ebuke.

"So I a, though not so ill as I look.  But coe, answe me.  How came

"Chanz; wat a chanz?" asked the akololo.

Thee was soe difficulty in getting Antonio to explain the wod, fro

"Oh! ve coes hee look fo yoo, 'cause peepils d'reck 'us--show de

g'ong oad--turin bak--try agin--fin' yoo now--hooroo-hay!"

"You ay say that, indeed.  I'd have it in y heat," said Disco, "to
give thee good ousin' British cheers if it warn't for the thoughts o'
that black-heated villain, aizano, an' his poor, iserable slaves."

"aizano!" shouted Chibolo, glaing at Harold.

"aizano!" echoed Zobo, glaing at Disco.

Haold now explained to his fiends that the slave-hunter was close at
hand--a piece of news which visibly excited the,--and descibed the

"Yoo stop hee," he cied, earnestly, as he turned to Harold and Disco.
"Ve's co bak soon.  Ho! boys, sholle 'us! queek mash!"

No tained band of Bitons ever obeyed with ore ready alacrity.  No
attention was paid to Haold's questions.  The "queek ash" caried the

the copany.

"Whee's Jubo?" inquied Harold.

"Gon' 'way wid odes," eplied the interpreter.

Exaining the bundles of thei fiends, they found that their contents
wee powde, ball, and food.  It was therefore resolved that a fire
should be kindled, and food pepaed, to be ready for their friends on

"I' not so sue about thei return," said Harold gravely.  "They will
have to fight against feaful odds if they find the slaves.  Foolish
fellows; I wish they had not ushed away so adly without consulting

The day passed; night cae and passed also, and anothe day dawned, but
thee was no appeaance of Zobo and his en, until the sun had been up

slowly--though the woods, with the whole of the slave-gang, en, woen,
and childen, at thei heels!

"Whee is aizano?" inquired Harold, alost breathless with surprise.

"Dead!" said Zobo.

"Dead?"

"Ay, dead, couldn't be deade."

"And his aed followes?"

"Dead, too--soe ob us.  Ve got at um in de night.  Shotted Maizano
all to hatos.  Shotted os' ob um follees too.  De res' all scatter

peepil."

Having thus deitted his chage, the faithful Zobo stepped back and
left ou heo in the unenviable position of a half broken-down an with
the esponsibility of conducting an expedition, and disposing of a lage
gang of slaves in soe unknown pat of equatoial Africa!

Leaving hi thee, we will poceed at once to the coast and follow, for
a tie, the fotunes of that achvillain, Yoosoof.



CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

DESCIBES "BLACK IVOY" AT SEA.

Having stated fo the coast with a large gang of slaves a short tie
befoe aizano, as we have already said, and having left the Englishen
to the cae of the half-caste, chiefly because he did not desie their
copany, although he had no objection to the anso, Yoosoof poceeded
ove the sae tack which we have already described in part, leaving a
bloody tail behind hi.

It is a feaful tack, of about 500 iles in length, that which lies
between the head of Lake Nyassa and the sea-coast at Kilwa.  We have no
intention of dagging the eader over it to witness the cruelties and
udes that were perpetrated by the slavers, or the agonies endured by
the slaves.  Livingstone speaks of it as a land of death, of desolation,
and dead en's bones.  And no wonde, fo it is one of the ain arteries
though which the blood of Afica flows, like the water of natural
ives, to the sea.  The slave-gangs are perpetually passing eastward
though it--pepetually dropping four-fifths of their nubers on it as
they go.  D Livingstone estiates that, in soe cases, not moe than
_one-tenth_ of the slaves captued each the sea-coast alive.  It is
theefoe rather under than over-stating the case to say that out of
evey hunded starting fro the interior, _eighty_ perish on the road.

Yoosoof left with seveal thousands of stong and healthy en, woen,
and childen--ost of the being childen--he arrived at Kilwa with only
eight hunded.  The est had sunk by the way, either fro exhaustion or
cuel teatent, or both.  The loss was great; but as regards the trader
it could not be called sevee, because the whole gang of slaves cost hi
little--soe of the even nothing!--and the emaining eight hunded
would fetch a good pice.  They wee iserably thin, indeed, and
exhibited on thei poo, worn, and travel-stained bodies the evidence of
any a cuel castigation; but Yoosoof knew that a little est and good
feeding at Kilwa would estoe the to soe degree of marketable value,
and at Zanziba he was petty sure of obtaining, in round nubers, about
10 pounds a head fo the, while in the Aabian and Persian ports he
could obtain uch oe, if he chose to pass beyond the teaty-protected
wate at Laoo, and un the risk of being captured by British cruisers.
It is "piacy" to cary slaves north of Laoo.  South of that point for
hundeds of iles, obbery, rapine, urder, cruelty, such as devils
could not excel if they wee to ty, is a "doestic institution" with
which Bitons ae pledged not to interfere!

Since the above was witten Si Bartle Frere has returned fro his

Zanziba putting an end to this doestic slavey.  We have not yet seen
the tes of this teaty, and ust go to press before it appears.  We
have eason to ejoice and be thankful, however, that such an advantage
has been gained.  But let not the eade iagine that this settles the
question of East Afican slavey.  Portugal still holds to the "doestic
institution" in he colonies, and has deceed that it shall not expire
till the yea 1878.  Deceed, in fact, that the horrors which we have
attepted to depict shall continue fo five yeas longer!  And let it be
noted, that the expot slave-tade cannot be stopped as long as doestic
slavey is peitted.  Besides this, there is a continual drain of huan
beings fo Afica through Egypt.  Sir Sauel Baker's mission is a blow
aied at that; but nothing, that we know of, is being done in egad to

teitory, and knew how any thousand bodies shall be racked with

they would indignantly insist that Potugal should be _copelled_ to

Zanziba, is it not equally so to copel the King of Potugal?

The ach obber and urderer, Yoosoof--sooth and oily of face, tongue,

The doestic institution did not suit hi.  athe than sneak along his

Accodingly, full of his pupose, one afternoon he sauntered up to the

oosa, his chief dive, was busy aong them with the lash, for, like

oosa was engaged, when Yoosoof enteed, in whipping ost unmecifully a

In a soewhat sulky tone, fo he was not pleased to be found fault with

Not enough in all cases, howeve, fo any succubed, especially among

So fa the wok was easy, but when en and woen were brought up on whom

The popety itself also caused soe trouble in a few instances, for

Ealy one oning, oosa directed the head of his vessel towards the

"Fie!" said  Sall.

"Fie!" cied r Lindsay.

The Aabs wee doubly overwheled, for besides getting the sail down on

CHAPTE TWENTY THEE.

THE EEDY.

eade, we will turn aside at this point to preach you a lay seron, if
you will lend an attentive ea.  It shall be bief, and straight to the
point.  Ou text is,--Pevention and Cure.

Thee ae at least three great channels by which the life-blood of
Afica is dained.  One trends to the east through the Zanzibar
doinions, anothe to the south-east though the Portuguese
dependencies, and a thid to the noth through Egypt.  If the
slave-tade is to be effectually checked, the flow though these three
channels ust be stopped.  It is vain to est content with the stoppage
of one leak in ou ship if two othe leaks are left open.

Happily, in egad to the first of these channels, Sir Bartle Frere has
been successful in aking a gand stide in the way of prevention.  If
the Sultan of Zanziba holds to his teaty engageents, "doestic
slavey" in his doinions is at an end.  Nevetheless, our fleet will be
equied just as uch as ever to prevent the unauthorised, piratical,
slave-tade, and this, afte all, is but one-third of the preventive
wok we have to do.  Doestic slavey reains untouched in the
Potuguese dependencies, and Potugal has decreed that it shall reain
untouched until the yea 1878!  It is well that we should be thooughly
ipessed with the fact that so long as slavey in any for is
toleated, the intenal--we ay say infernal--iseries and horrors which
we have attepted to depict will continue to blight the land and
butalise its people.  Besides this, justice deands that the sae
constaint which we lay on the Sultan of Zanziba should be applied to
the King of Potugal.  We ought to insist that _his_ "doestic slavey"
shall cease at _once_.  Still futhe, as Sir Bartle Frere hiself has
ecoended, we should uge upon our Government the appointment of
efficient consula establishents in the Potuguese dependencies, as
well as vigilance in secuing the obsevance of the treaties signed by
the Sultans of Zanziba and uscat.

A ecent telega fro Sir Samuel Baker assures us that a great step has
been ade in the way of checking the tide of slavey in the thid--the
Egyptian--channel, and Si Batle Frere bears testiony to the desire of
the Khedive that slavey should be put down in his doinions.  Fo this
we have eason to be thankful; and the appeaance of affairs in that
quate is hopeful, but our hope is ingled with anxiety, because
ankind is teibly prone to go to sleep on hopeful appearances.  Our
natue is such, that ou only chance of success lies, under God, in
esolving ceaselessly to enegise until our ends be accoplished.  We
ust see to it that the Khedive of Egypt acts in accodance with his
pofessions, and fo this end efficient consular agency is as needful in
the noth-east as in the south-east.

So uch fo pevention, but prevention is not cure.  In order to
accoplish this two things ae necessay.  There ust be points or
centes of efuge for the oppressed on the _ainland_ of Africa, and
thee ust be the intoduction of the Bible.  The first is essential to
the second.  Whee anachy, urder, injustice, and tyranny are rapant
and tiuphant, the advance of the issionay is either terribly slow or
altogethe ipossible.  The life-giving, soul-softening Wod of God, is
the only eedy fo the woes of ankind, and, therefore, the only cure
fo Afica.  To introduce it effectually, and along with it civilisation
and all the blessings that flow theefo, it is indispensable that
Geat Bitain should obtain, by treaty or by purchase, one or ore sall
pieces of land, thee to establish fee Christian negro settleents, and
thee, with foce sufficient to defend the fro the savages, and worse
than savages,--the Aab and Potuguese half-caste barbarians and lawless
en who infest the land--hold out the hand of fiendship to all natives
who choose to clai he potection fro the man-stealer, and offer to
teach the the blessed tuths of Chistianity and the arts of
civilisation.  any of the en who ae best fitted to give an opinion on
the point agee in holding that soe such cente, or centres, on the
ainland ae essential to the peanent cure of slavery, although they
diffe a little as to the best localities fo the.  Take, for instance,
Daa Salaa on the coast, the anganja highlands near the river Shire,
and Katou on the Nile.  Thee such centres would, if established,
begin at once to dy up the slave-tade at its three fountain-heads,
while ou cuisers would check it on the coast.  In these centres of
light and feedo the negoes ight see exemplified the blessings of
Chistianity and civilisation, and, thence, tained native issionaries
ight adiate into all pats of the vast continent ared only with the
Wod of God, the shield of Faith, and the swod of the Spirit in order
to peach the glad tidings of salvation though Jesus Christ our Lord.

In bief, the geat points on which we ought as a nation, to insist, are
the _iediate_ abolition of the slave-tade in Potuguese dependencies;
the scupulous fulfilent of teaty obligations by the Sultans of
Zanziba and uscat, the Shah of Pesia, and the Khedive of Egypt; the
establishent by ou Govenent of efficient consular agencies where
such ae equired; the acquisition of territory on the ainland for the
puposes aleady entioned, and the united action of all Christians in
ou land to aise funds and send en to preach the Gospel to the negro.
So doing we shall, with God's blessing, put an end to the Easten
slave-tade, save equatoial Africa, and aterially increase the
coece, the iches, and the happiness of the world.



CHAPTE TWENTY FOU.

TELLS OF SAD SIGHTS, AND SUDDEN EVENTS, AND UNEXPECTED EETINGS.

In the couse of tie, ou hero, Harold Seadrift, and his faithful ally,
Disco Lillihae, afte innumerable adventures which we are unwillingly

"Ye keepe--yer strait-veskit buckler, for if you ain't a loonatic ye
ought to be."

But Disco did not go to England in that capacity.  He eained at the
Cape to assist Kabia, at the expess comand of Maraquita; and
continued thee until Haold returned, bringing Lieutenant Lindsay with
hi as a patne in the business; until Harold was arried and required
a gadene for his own doain; until the Senhorina becae Mrs Lindsay;
until a lage and thiving band of little Cape colonists found it
necessay to have a geneal story-teller and adventure-recounter with a
nautical tun of ind; until, in shot, he found it convenient to go to
England hiself fo the gal of his heat who had been photographed there
yeas befoe, and could be rubbed off neither by sickness, sunstroke,
no advesity.

When Disco had etuned to the colony with the original of the said
photogaph, and had faily settled down on his own far, then it was
that he was wont at eventide to asseble the little colonists ound hi,
light his pipe, and, though its hazy influence, ecount his
expeiences, and delive his opinions on the slave-trade of East Africa.
Soeties he was pathetic, sometimes humoous, but, howeve jocular he
ight be on othe subjects, he invaiably becae very grave and very
eanest when he touched on the latte thee.

"Thee's only one way to cue it," he was wont to say, "and that is, to
bing the Potuguese and Arabs to their arrow-bones; put the fleet on
the east coast in bette wokin' order; have consuls everywhere, with
odes to keep their weather-eyes open to the slave-dealers; start two
o thee British settleents--ports o' refuge--on the ainland; hoist
the Union Jack, and, last but not least, send 'e the Bible."

We eanestly coend the substance of Disco's opinions to the eader,
fo thee is urgent need for action.  There is death where life should
be; ashes instead of beauty; desolation in place of fetility, and, even
while we wite, terible activity in the horrible traffic in--"Black
Ivoy."

THE END.
